#### Machine learning
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Narrator] Back in the day, a machine was just a machine.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=5)** It did whatever machine things it did, like stamping metal, or turning a propeller, or maybe washing your clothes with a fair amount of help on your part.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=14)** But nowadays, machines have to do more than just their given mechanical function.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=21)** Now, a washing machine's supposed to be smart.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=24)** It's supposed to learn about you, and how you like your clothes, and it's supposed to adjust its functions according to its sensors, and it's supposed to send you a gentle message on your phone when it's all done taking care of everything.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=37)** This is a big change, not just for washing machines, but for so many other machines, and for data science processes as well.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=49)** This gets to the issue of machine learning.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=52)** And a very simple definition of that is the ability of algorithms to learn from data, and to learn in such a way that they can improve their function in the future.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=67)** Now, learning is a pretty universal thing.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=69)** Here's how humans learn.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=72)** Humans, memorization is hard.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=74)** I know this. I teach.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=76)** And memorization is something my students struggle with every semester.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=81)** On the other hand, spotting patterns is often pretty easy for humans, as is reacting well and adaptively to new situations that resemble the old ones in many but not all ways.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=95)** On the other hand, the way that machines learn is a little bit different.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=100)** Unlike humans, memorization is really easy for machines.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=104)** You can give them a million digits, it'll remember it perfectly and give it right back to you.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=110)** But for a machine, for an algorithm, spotting patterns in terms of, "Here's a visual pattern."
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=117)** "Here's a pattern over time."
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=119)** Those are much harder for algorithms.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=122)** And new situations can be very challenging for algorithms to take what they learned previously, and adapt it to something that may differ in a few significant ways.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=134)** But the general idea is that once you figure out how machines learn, and the ways that you can work with that, you can do some useful things.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=142)** So for instance, there's the spam email, and you get a new email, and the algorithm can tell whether it's a spam.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=150)** I use a couple of different email providers.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=152)** And I will tell you, some of them are much better at this than others.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=158)** There's also image identification, for instance, telling whether this is a human face, or whose face it is.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=166)** Or there's the translation of languages, where you enter text either written or spoken, and it translates it back.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=175)** A very complicated task for humans, but something that machines have learned how to do much better than they used to.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=182)** It's still not a hundred percent, but getting closer all the time.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=187)** Now, the important thing here, is that you're not specifying all the criteria in each of these examples, and you're not laying out a giant collection of, "if this, then that" statement's in a flowchart.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=200)** That would be something called an expert system.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=202)** Those were created several decades ago, and have been found to have limited utility.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=207)** And they're certainly not responsible for the modern developments of machine learning.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=213)** Instead, a more common approach, really is to just teach your machine.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=218)** You train it.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=219)** And the way you do that, is you show the algorithm millions of labeled examples.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=224)** If you're trying to teach it to identify photos of cats versus other animals, you give it millions of photos, and you say, "This is a cat, this is cat, this is not, this is not, this is."
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=236)** And then the algorithm finds its own distinctive features that are consistent across at least many of the examples, of cats.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=245)** Now, what's important here, is that the features, the things in the pictures that the algorithm latches onto, may not be relevant to humans.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=256)** We look at things like the eyes, and the whiskers, and the nose and the ears.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=259)** It might be looking at the curve on the outside of the cheek, relevant to the height of one ear to another.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=265)** It might be looking just at a small patch of lines around the nose.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=271)** Those may not be the things that humans latch onto.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=274)** And then sometimes they're not even visible to humans.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=277)** It turns out that algorithms can find things that are very subtle, pixel by pixel changes and images or very faint sounds and audio patches, or individual letters and texts, and it can respond to those.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=294)** That's both a blessing and a curse.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=296)** It means that it can find things that humans don't.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=298)** But it also can react in strange ways occasionally.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=302)** But once you take all this training, you give your algorithm millions of labels and examples, and it starts classifying things.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=311)** Well, then you want to use something like a neural network, which has been responsible for the major growth in machine learning and data science in the past five years or so.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=323)** These diagrams here are different layouts of possible neural networks that go from the left to the right.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=331)** Some of them circle around, or they return back to where they were.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=335)** But all of these are different ways of taking the information and processing it.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=339)** Now, the theory of neural networks or artificial neural networks has existed for years.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=345)** The theory is not new.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=346)** What's different, however, is that computing power has recently caught up to the demands that the theory places, and in addition, the availability labeled data, primarily thanks to social media, has recently caught up too.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=362)** And so now we have this perfect combination.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=364)** The theory has existed, but the computing power, and the raw data that it needs, have both arrived to make it possible to do these computations, that in many ways resemble what goes on in the human brain, and then allow it to think creatively about the data, find its own patterns, and label things.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=386)** Now, I do want to say something about the relationship between data science and machine learning.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=392)** Data science can definitely be done without machine learning.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=397)** Any traditional classification task, logistic regression, decision tree, that's not usually machine learning, and it's very effective data science.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=407)** Most predictive models, or even something like a sentiment analysis of social media texts.
>
> **[6:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=413)** On the other hand, machine learning without data science, well, you know, not so much.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=418)** It's possible to do machine learning without extensive domain expertise.
>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=422)** So that's one element of data science.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=424)** On the other hand, you would nearly always want to do this in collaboration with some sort of topical expert.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=430)** Mostly, I like to think of machine learning as a sub-discipline of data science.
>
> **[7:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=436)** And that just brings up one more thing I want to say.
>
> **[7:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=439)** The neural networks, and the deep learning neural networks in particular that have been responsible for nearly all of these amazing developments in machine learning are a little bit of a black box, which means it's hard to know exactly what the algorithm is looking at, or how it's processing the data.
>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=455)** And one result of that is it kind of limits your ability to interpret what's going on, even though the predictions and classifications can be amazingly accurate.
>
> **[7:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/machine-learning?u=76281980&t=465)** I'll say more about neural networks and these issues elsewhere, but they highlight the trade-offs, the potential and the compromises that are inherent in some of these really exciting developments that have been taking place in one extraordinary influential part of the data science world.

#### Deep learning and neural networks
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A light switch is a simple everyday object.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=3)** It turns things on, it turns them off.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=6)** The neurons in your brain are also on/off switches.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=11)** They get turned on.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=12)** And when that happens, an electrical impulse travels down the axon and possibly turns on other neurons.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=20)** And when you have a whole bunch of switches, say 100 billion of them like the neurons in your head, and you get them all connected, then amazing things can happen, like love and consciousness.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=32)** And in data science, an artificial neural network where the functioning is loosely inspired by our biological neural networks can also give rise to surprising things.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=42)** Examples of this include autonomous cars that can drive themselves safely, or medical diagnostics where it can help diagnose diseases in MRIs and EKGs, or the ability to find photos without you ever tagging them.

>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=81)** But to get back to our original question, what exactly is a deep learning neural network?

>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=103)** So imagine, for example, that you want to create a neural network that can categorize what's in a photo.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=110)** You take a digital photo and you're going to get the X and Y coordinates of each pixel in the photo along with its red, green, and blue components.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=122)** So those are five numbers for each pixel, and you probably have a lot of pixels in your photo, but that information goes into the first or input layer of the neural network.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=135)** And then maybe what happens is it takes that information and it starts combining it in ways that, and if we want to think about it as the way a human might do it, and the neural network doesn't necessarily, but you could think of it as the first step is it finds lines in the image and then from there, it does some more processing and it finds the edges or outlines of objects, and then from there, it identifies the shapes that are in the photo, and then maybe at that point it can give you an output and say what it is a picture of.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=170)** And so, it's sequential processing.

>

>

>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=373)** There are also efficient neural networks, and what that refers to is the energy requirements for training and running neural networks can sometimes be overwhelming.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=385)** I mean, they use a huge amount of power.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=388)** And so methods are being developed to try to reduce the energy demands and the computing demands of neural networks.
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=396)** Part of that is showing up at the use of neural networks or variations on the machine learning in embedded devices, little sensors, you know, things that are like in your smoke detector where it can be smart enough to tell that the house is on fire from smoke from your toaster or something else going on.
>
> **[6:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=414)** But embedding machine learning into very low powered, very limited devices is another exciting area of development.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=425)** There's also hybrid AI, which is in part going back to the idea of what's called good old fashioned AI, which is back in the 50s when people tried to develop systems that encoded expert knowledge of a domain.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=440)** But there's an attempt to use neural networks that draw at least in part on expert knowledge to make them more functional within a particular domain.
>
> **[7:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=449)** And then that might be related to what is also called liquid neural networks.
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=454)** And these are flexible algorithms that keep developing in response to new data, not just the training data where they first learned how to do things because most deep learning neural networks are kind of set in stone once they've been trained.
>
> **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=467)** They just kind of operate after that.
>
> **[7:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=470)** But the liquid neural networks are more flexible.
>
> **[7:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=473)** And one interesting consequence of that is the cause and effect relationships that can arise from the responsiveness can also make them more interpretable and more explainable.
>
> **[8:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/deep-learning-and-neural-networks?u=76281980&t=484)** And so, there's a lot of overlap between data science, between artificial intelligence, and also the power of deep learning neural networks and the massive explosive growth going on that field and how it can be applied within data science.

#### Transformers and attention for generative AI
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=40)** Or can you go one way or two ways?
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=44)** Traditional neural networks go in just one direction, going from input to output, forwards one step at a time.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=53)** It's a one way set of calculations.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=55)** And the calculations at point A only influence things at point B.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=61)** So for example, a recurrent neural network that was trying to write a sentence would go forward one word at a time without ever changing or going back to look again at earlier words.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=73)** On the other hand, there are times when the meaning of one word will depend on context, including things that can come later in a sentence.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=81)** If I were to talk about Python in this course, you could reasonably assume that I'm talking about a programming language, unless I suddenly start talking about snake skin.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=90)** When that happens, well, it lets you know that it changes your initial impression of what I mentioned earlier.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=98)** And for that reason, a two-way flow of attention and meaning can make a big difference.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=103)** And that's one of the major contributions of the attention mechanism that underlies transformers and generative AI in general.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=112)** So in a neural network, data flows in one direction from input on the left through hidden layers in the middle, to output on the right.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=121)** And as a note, this particular graphic is designed to suggest a long short-term memory, or LSTM model, which is the kind of neural network.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=130)** And until recently, this was the state-of-the-art for sequential data, like translating languages or generating text.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=137)** Transformers work a little differently.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=140)** Input is fed into a series of stacked encoder modules.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=144)** You see that coming from the top left and going down.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=148)** These connect to a stack of decoder modules on the right leading to the output, and it's these decoder modules that generate the output.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=160)** And all of this relies on something called attention or the attention mechanism.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=164)** That's how transformers run.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=166)** This approach relates each word in a sentence to every other word.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=170)** So for instance, I have a sentence on the left here, that says, bikes are fun.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=174)** I love riding mine.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=176)** And then the word mine at the bottom, the question is, well, what is it referring to there?
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=182)** And what the attention mechanism is going to do is it's going to see how likely that is to refer to other things in the sentence.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=191)** Words with stronger associations get more attention.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=194)** And in this case, bikes at the top is going to be the strongest mine.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=199)** Give that some.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=200)** It's the same word though, and I might get some.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=202)** And it doesn't give as much attention to anything else.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=205)** And this is how the attention mechanism selects things that are earlier in the sequence to help generate the later output.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=214)** Now, there are some advantages to this.
>

>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=227)** So not just the previous word of the previous sentence, but maybe the previous a hundred pages of stuff that you put in.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=234)** Also, it works faster than other approaches like recurrent neural networks, that's RNN's.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=239)** Or long short-term memory models, that's LTSM.

>

>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=253)** So there are some huge advantages.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/transformers-and-attention-for-generative-ai?u=76281980&t=255)** And really the big one is that all of this makes generative AI possible.

> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/big-data?u=76281980&t=309)** It's everywhere.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/big-data?u=76281980&t=311)** It has simply become the new normal for data.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/big-data?u=76281980&t=315)** The practices that it introduced, the techniques that it made possible are used every single day now in the data science world.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/big-data?u=76281980&t=323)** And so, while it's possible to separate big data and data science, the two become so integrated now that big data is simply taken for granted as an element of the new normal in the data world.

#### Predictive analytics
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Speaker] When a person is convicted of a crime, a judge has to decide what the appropriate response is and how that might help bring about positive outcomes.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=11)** One interesting thing that can contribute to that is what's called restorative justice.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=16)** This is a form of justice that focuses on repair to the harm done as opposed to punishment, and it often involves at the judge's discretion and the victim's desire mediation between the victim and the offender.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=31)** Now, one of the interesting things about this is it's a pretty easy procedure and it has some very significant outcomes.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=39)** Participation in restorative justice predicts improved outcomes on all of the following.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=47)** People feel that they were able to tell their story and that their opinion was considered.

> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=54)** They feel that the judge or mediator was fair.

>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=94)** And so when a judge is trying to make a decision, this is one thing they can keep in mind in trying to predict a particular outcome.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=101)** Now, in the world of predictive analytics where you're using data to try to predict outcomes, the restorative justice is a very simple one based on simple analyses within data science and predictive analytics, you'll see more complicated things like for instance, whether a person is more likely to click on a particular button or make a purchase based on a particular offer.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=124)** You're going to see medical researchers looking at things that can predict the risk of a disease, as well as the responsiveness of particular treatments.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=133)** You'll also look at things like the classification of photos and what's being predicted there is whether a machine can accurately predict what a human would do if they did the same particular task.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=145)** These are all major topics within the field of predictive analytics, but it's important to remember that predictions fundamentally are associations, not cause and effect relationships.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=159)** So for instance, here's a spurious correlation.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=162)** It's the correlation across the year between ice cream sales, which you see, going from near zero in January up to a peak in July and back down near zero in December, and shark attacks following a very similar pattern, and it's called spurious because this isn't a cause and effect.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=182)** It doesn't mean that sharks cause ice cream sales or ice cream brings out sharks.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=186)** They just both go up during the summer in the Northern Hemisphere when it's hot and people go out to the beach and things like that.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=194)** And so this is an association that is not particularly meaningful, and there are huge numbers of examples of these kinds of associations that can be used to predict but don't have a meaningful association, and that's something you need to be aware of.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=213)** On the other hand, it may be that sometimes that's enough.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=218)** If all you need to know is what to expect, what to predict, then predictions are sufficient.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=224)** There was a very well known paper from 2009 called "The Unreasonable Effectiveness of Data" by Halevy, Norvig, and Pereira, which really was at the beginning of the data science revolution and said, "When you have web scale data, when you have billions of cases, you are dealing with correlational data.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=245)** But it is so precise that it is functionally causal."
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=250)** Now that's an interesting philosophical stand, and I'm not going to get really involved in that except to say that sometimes if really all you're trying to do is predict and you can make extremely precise predictions based on the very large and diverse data set that you have, again, maybe that's all you need.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=268)** And let's also think a little bit about the models that are involved in predictive analytics.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=274)** Because they can vary dramatically in their complexity.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=277)** You can have very elementary models.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=280)** Seriously, a time series with a hand drawn bar charts might be enough to let you know that, "Oh, next year we expect things to go up 10%."
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=288)** Again, it might be enough.

> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=290)** Or you can get to things that are more sophisticated, like machine learning, which humans program, but humans can't necessarily follow the data that goes all the way through it.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=300)** But it does make very good predictions.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=302)** Again, what would a human do in this particular case?
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=305)** And the algorithm is able to estimate that with a high-degree of accuracy.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=310)** And then the most complex at this exact moment are the generative AI methods, which can be used to make predictions, but there's a whole other level of inscrutability about them.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=323)** Use the method that is best suited to your particular use case.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=329)** An elementary method is fine, only go past that if you need something more to make predictions that are more refined, more nuanced, but use whatever is most applicable for getting the actionable insights you need in your particular data situation.

#### Prescriptive analytics
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes you just have to do the impossible.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=5)** About 2,500 years ago, the Greek philosopher Zeno of Elea argued that it was impossible to get from point A to point B, like walking across your room.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=15)** His reasoning was that before you could get all the way to point B, you first had to get halfway there.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=21)** And before you could get the rest of the way, you had to go halfway again.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=25)** The process of getting halfway would occur an infinite number of times, which Zeno said was impossible, so you could never get to point B.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=35)** Now, aside from the fact that Zeno didn't know that you could solve an infinite series problem with calculus, the obvious answer is that people walk from one part of the room to the other all the time.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=47)** So the theoretically impossible task was obviously possible and accomplished frequently.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=54)** And that gets us to an interesting issue about cause and effect relationships.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=59)** Now, strictly speaking, three things need to happen to be able to say that one thing causes another.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=66)** The first is that there needs to be an observed correlation between the putative cause and the effect.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=74)** That is, the effect needs to be more likely when the cause is present.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=78)** If it's not, it can't possibly cause it.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=81)** The second thing is temporal precedence, and that simply means that the cause needs to come before the effect if it's going to be a cause.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=89)** And both of those are pretty easy to establish.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=93)** The first one, you just need something like a correlation coefficient.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=95)** The second one, you just need to show that the cause happened first.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=99)** You can set that up pretty easily.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=101)** But the third one's the kicker.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=103)** No other explanations for the association between the possible cause and effect.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=110)** The connection between those two can't be accounted for by anything else.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=114)** The problem is that part is theoretically impossible.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=118)** You can't show that there's nothing else there.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=120)** And so while we go along pretty well with number one, number two.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=124)** Three is this huge sticking point.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=126)** On the other hand, that doesn't mean you can't establish causality.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=131)** It means you just kind of have to get close enough for practical purposes.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=136)** Now, let me go back and compare what I'm talking about here with cause and effect to something we've seen previously.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=142)** I've spoken about predictive analytics.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=145)** That is where you're focusing on correlations, because correlations are adequate for saying, "if this happens, then this will probably happen as well."
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=155)** And there's a huge amount of work in data science on predictive analytics, and really amazing things have come out of that.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=163)** On the other hand, prescriptive analytics is about causation.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=168)** You're trying to specifically focus on things that you can do to make something happen that's important to you.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=176)** Now, the gold standard for establishing cause and effect is what's called an RCT, or a randomized controlled trial.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=185)** Theoretically, they're very simple.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=187)** You assign a bunch of people to one situation or another.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=190)** You do that randomly.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=191)** You control all the other conditions and then you see how things come out.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=195)** Theoretically, very simple to do, but I can tell you, given my training as an experimental research psychologist, they can be enormously difficult, often complex in practice.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=207)** And so the theory is nice and clean, but the practice can be really difficult.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=212)** There is one major exception to that, and that's A/B testing for web design, where, for instance, you set up your software to have one offer on this version and another offer on another version of your website, and you see which one gets more clicks.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=227)** That can be automated, and it is an experimental design and it's randomized.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=231)** It's an example of what we're looking for, even though that's a very simple one.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=236)** But something more complex like that, like for instance, does making public transportation in a city have a direct effect on the influx of new businesses?
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=245)** That's a huge experiment.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=247)** That's very, very difficult to do well.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=250)** And so the gold standard is the randomized controlled trial, but often very difficult to do in reality.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=256)** And that leads you to some of the more practical solutions, the alternatives that can help you get close to a cause and effect conclusion, even if they can't get you 100% of the way.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=267)** Those include things like what-if simulations.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=270)** These are ways of manipulating data in a spreadsheet.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=273)** They say, "well, if this is true, and if we have these parameters, then what will we expect?"
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=277)** And then you can simply see how that matches up with reality a little bit later.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=281)** You can do optimization models.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=283)** These are correlational models based on the information you have, that say, "if we balance things out so we spend a certain amount of time and money on this, or if we price things in a particular way, that will maximize an outcome."
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=295)** Again, it's correlational, but it often gives you specific suggestions on what to do based on that past information.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=303)** You can do what are called cross-lag correlations.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=306)** This is where you have data at two or more specific points in time, and you're able to see if changes in the cause at time one produce corresponding changes in the effect at time two, and not vice versa.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=321)** And then finally, there's the entire category of what are called quasi-experiments.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=326)** These are a whole host of research designs that let you use correlational data to try to estimate the size of the causal relationship between two variables.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=338)** On the other hand, one of the easiest ways to isolate causality is simply to do things again and again.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=347)** Iteration is critical.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=349)** You may be familiar with this chart, which comes from the agile design process.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=353)** You design, you develop, you try something out once.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=357)** Well, test it and then do it again.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=359)** Make a variation, do it again.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=361)** Make a variation.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=362)** And as you do that, you will come close enough to causality through your repeated experience that you'll then be able to isolate and say, "this particular action is producing the cause that we want."
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=375)** That is the prescriptive analysis.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=378)** That's the result that you're looking for.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=381)** But the ready availability of generative AI has also had an impact on causal inference, and by extension, prescriptive analytics.
>
> **[6:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=390)** As an example, here's a collection of papers on generative AI and causal inference that have been published on arXiv, a research paper repository.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=400)** They include general causal inference, causal EGM, causal representation, learning large language models, and causal inference and collaboration.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=409)** And all of these have were published subsequent to the release of ChatGPT in November of 2022.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=416)** There's a huge amount of work going on right there.
>
> **[6:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=419)** And these methods that are in these papers rely on something called encoding generative modeling, or EGM, which allows high dimensional data, meaning many, many, many, many variables, to be encoded or represented in a more compact form.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=435)** So we have data compression and synthesis, which takes less storage and is easier to work with.
>
> **[7:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=441)** It also allows for the generation of synthetic data that matches higher dimensional structure.
>
> **[7:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=448)** It allows for simulation abilities.
>
> **[7:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=450)** So you can take something that might have a low dimensional form, like a two dimensional object, and use it to give you an idea of a larger, higher dimensional representation, like portraying a story with shadow puppets.
>
> **[7:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=463)** Also, these methods allow you to use natural language input and output.
>
> **[7:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=470)** You can talk to the computer and tell it what you're looking for, and you can actually engage in a discussion with it, and you can have it explain the output.
>
> **[7:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=478)** And so the use of generative AI, even in causal modeling and prescriptive analytics, contains a huge amount of potential for vastly more efficient and productive work.
>
> **[8:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=490)** That said, generative AI can help, and it can build on what's in the data.
>
> **[8:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=494)** But if, for example, there's bias in the data, then generative AI might simply amplify that bias.
>
> **[8:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=500)** And chat mechanisms can make the analysis accessible, but there's no guarantee that it will latch onto the elements that are most important in your work, in your project.
>
> **[8:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=509)** So with prescriptive analytics, as with everything else, keep your wits about you.
>
> **[8:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=515)** And remember that critical thinking still matters, even when you have these very powerful algorithms doing a lot of the grunt work for you.
>
> **[8:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/prescriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=525)** And to get back to our original point, causality may be theoretically philosophically impossible, but prescriptive analytics can get you close enough for practical purposes.

### 4. 3. Ethics, Privacy, and Regulation

#### Bias
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] You're doing wonderful technical work.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=2)** You're working with a great computer.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=3)** You got good code, and anyhow, sometimes things just glitch and things fall apart, and it just kind of explodes on you.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=10)** Things can go wrong in data science like they can everywhere else, and there are really too many examples to list.
>

>

>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=51)** Now, some of these are due to technical issues.
>

>

>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=67)** There also may have been statistical artifacts from small samples.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=71)** You can get a correlation in a small sample that's not there in the overall group.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=76)** Also, you can focus on overall accuracy and ignore the important differences between subgroups.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=83)** These are technical issues, and they can be dealt with a lot of times in a technical way.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=89)** There are also, however, failures that really have to do more about the people carrying out the project.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=95)** There's the failure to gather a diverse training set that does represent all the people, all the situations that your algorithm might encounter.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=103)** There's the failure to capture diversity of data labels, knowing that what is in this picture?
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=109)** Is this a good thing or a bad thing?
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=111)** And that varies from person to person, but it also varies from one culture or group to another.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=119)** And there's the failure to use more flexible algorithms.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=123)** It can look not just at large-scale, macro, overall patterns, but at the more micro, nuanced, individual subgroups and exceptional cases.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=133)** One of the consequences that this can lead to self-fulfilling prophecies.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=137)** So for instance, there is research that shows that women get shown job ads for lower paying jobs.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=145)** And so a woman is shown ads for lower paying jobs, so she's more likely to apply for those and get one of those lower paying jobs.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=152)** And her job is now taken as data into the algorithm that makes the recommendations, and it just kind of reinforces and exacerbates the problem.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=161)** And so this is one where a lack of awareness about how the algorithms work can, in fact, create the problem that maybe you wanted to avoid in the first place and that you should have avoided.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=173)** Now, there are a few things that you can do.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=174)** Number one is you can, of course, check for biased output, and this requires having judges from a very wide range of perspectives to look at the data.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=184)** You can consult with all parties.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=186)** The people who are going to be using the algorithm, the people whose lives will be affected by it, they should be included in the evaluation of the algorithm itself.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=195)** And of course, include diversity, not just demographic diversity and cultural diversity, but lots of different methods, lots of different ways of looking at the data, and evaluating the impact of your work.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=207)** Now, this is all true for predictive AI, which is generally trying to predict how a human would act in the same situation.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=214)** But generative AI introduces even greater risks.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=219)** So for instance, when making images or writing texts, there's the risk of either overrepresenting or underrepresenting different groups.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=228)** You don't want to end up with a whole bunch of clones, and you don't want to have algorithms that specifically avoid things that people know historically to be true.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=237)** And there's also the fact that generative AI, while it's based on amazing programming and amazing mathematical calculations, is frequently really bad at doing math on its own.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=246)** And it's subject to hallucinations, where it says things that were not there in the training, so that then it makes things up.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=253)** And so you got to be really careful with that.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=256)** And then finally, because it's a computer and because these algorithms are coming from these big technical organizations, they tend to encourage a lot of trust and arguably, too much trust in certain situations, where people should keep their wits about them.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=270)** They should be a little more skeptical from time to time about what the algorithm is giving them.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=276)** You know, then there's the effect of people realizing that the text or audio that you sent to them are generated by AI, usually because things seem a little off.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=287)** And aside from not being professional quality, I mean, really people expect better.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=292)** It leads to an erosion of trust.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=293)** If you messed up in this one very simple thing, how do we know that we can trust you in things that are more important?
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=300)** And that kind of trust is very difficult to rebuild, so be cautious with what you do.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bias?u=76281980&t=308)** And if you want to learn more about this, we have another course here at the LinkedIn Learning Library on AI accountability essential training that addresses a lot of the issues that come up in data science and AI, and how you can better anticipate them, prepare for them, and respond to them, so you can then use AI in a more effective way to get the insight you need from your work without bringing in new, unexpected, and potentially troublesome difficulties.

#### Security and privacy

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Your data has a lot of value to it all on its own, and so does your work on your data.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=7)** The challenge, of course, is that value sometimes attracts people who don't have your own best interests at heart.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=16)** So, for instance, sometimes people will come and steal your datasets.

>

>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=49)** Any one of these is a major security risk to a data science project.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=55)** And it's frustrating enough when it's thing like trying to predict what the weather's going to be.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=60)** But think about it when it's a high-stakes outcome.
>
