>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=71)** The idea there is what could go wrong if somebody is either trying to take what you have or deliberately mess with you.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=80)** Well, it turns out that the general phrase for this in the machine learning world is adversarial attacks.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=88)** That's when an adversary, somebody who's working against you, attacks your system and they are attempts to either bypass your system or corrupt your system, your machine learning algorithm.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=99)** Now, there are several people who will do this, and not all of them are bad guys.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=104)** So, for one thing, there are privacy activists.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=107)** These are people who work with machine learning the same way you do when you, like, maybe turn on the privacy protections on your phone or on your web browser.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=116)** There are also people who are trying to conceal illegal or unethical behavior, or maybe frame somebody else, or maybe they're trying to interfere with a competitor's performance, or gain an advantage.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=127)** Or maybe they're trying to get past security settings and access users like a spam messages or turning your computer into a spambot or a blockchain zombie.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=138)** Or maybe they're trying to hack into the system and steal or possibly worse manipulate data, which in and of itself can be an act of cyber terrorism.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=148)** But let me discuss two different kinds of adversarial attacks regardless of the motivation.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=152)** The first one is what's called an evasion attack.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=155)** Think of it as somebody putting a mask on.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=158)** This is where a model already exists.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=161)** Say, for instance, a facial recognition system, and it's an attack on that implemented model by trying to get around it.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=169)** Sometimes, the people doing this will create new examples that fool the algorithm, and, for instance, make it think that somebody else.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=178)** I know this is a silly example right here with this mask, but there are ways to do it very subtly to print glass frames that kind of look silly hodgepodge that make the system think that you are a specific other person.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=192)** Also, evasion and text can be done with images, that can be done with video or audio, or even something like a text-classifying system.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=200)** Now the good news is evasion attacks can usually be overcome and defeated by updating the algorithm.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=206)** You've got this new information, you know what's happening, you can adapt to it.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=211)** On the other hand, there are what are called poisoning attacks.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=214)** Think of it as something that's completely corroding and corrupting your algorithm.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=219)** This is an attack on a model that is in development before it's been implemented.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=224)** Now, that's harder to do, but what it does is it embeds.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=228)** It bakes in the mistakes into the algorithm.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=232)** And the problem here is that algorithms that are frequently updated and which therefore are less susceptible to evasion attacks are in fact more susceptible to poisoning attacks.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=245)** And so security becomes a major issue, especially when you're dealing with significant outcomes.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=252)** Things like financial systems, things like health systems, things like military systems.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=259)** It's going to make a very big difference there.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=262)** And then there are some special issues that are associated with generative AI.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=266)** So, for instance, your data might be incorporated into AI training datasets without your awareness.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=275)** Now, that's one thing if you're asking for recipes and directions to something, but it's a whole other thing if you're using sensitive, personally identifiable information, or other proprietary data.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=286)** Your data can get sucked up like a vacuum into the training dataset.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=289)** So, you will want to make sure that you're using a secure version of AI when you are interacting with it.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=298)** The second is that there's a risk of using bad code or sources.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=302)** Generative AI has generated a lot of bad code.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=305)** Sometimes mixing programming languages.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=307)** Sometimes using obsolete or unusual functions that obviously weren't written by somebody who's fluent with that particular program, so on.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=315)** So, some code-sharing sites have had to ban n AI code because of these kinds of quality issues, and you're going to want to pay special attention to the code it generates for your own use or your organization's use.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=328)** And then finally, the work can be difficult to maintain if you didn't write it and if no human in your organization did.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=335)** It can sometimes be hard to know what went into the project, how it works, what its dependencies are, and how to update it incrementally.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=343)** So, you can save a lot of time by using the generative AI to get started, but it does come with certain long-term costs, and it does come with certain long-term security risks.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=356)** And so, you know, what can you do about it?
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=358)** Well, there are at least a few options.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=360)** One, of course, is to show a little less trust and have a little more security effort.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=367)** I'm a particularly trusting person, but I understand the need for this in many situations.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=374)** Another one is what's called a white hat evaluation.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=376)** That's where you hire a hacker and try to get them to break into your system or try to corrupt it and see what it takes.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=384)** So, they share that with you and then you can make the changes necessary.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=388)** Also, regular testing of algorithms.
>
> **[6:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=390)** Are they in fact working the way you expect them to?
>
> **[6:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=393)** Just think of it as a periodic checkup on the performance of the model.
>
> **[6:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=398)** And then, of course, there are laws and regulations that both govern the security of algorithms, their performance, and attacks against them.
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=406)** All of these things together can make for a more secure environment where you can conduct your machine learning and your data science and get your outcomes without having to get so worried about getting tripped up in the process.
>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/security-and-privacy?u=76281980&t=422)** You want to bring the value without bringing the drama.

#### Legal
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Data science can make you feel like a superhero who's here to save the world, or at least your business world.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=8)** But an alarming amount of data work can also end up in court or on the wrong side of a protest.

> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=14)** So I want to talk about a few things that can help keep you, your company and your data science work on the up and up.

>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=41)** In the United States, there are laws like HIPAA and FERPA: the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act, and the Family Education Rights and Privacy Act, which have a lot to do with records that can be used in your analysis, information that can be shared.

> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=58)** There was also the proposed American Privacy Rights Act, which would be a nationwide privacy law; it was proposed in '24, but didn't pass.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=67)** In the European Union, there's the General Data Protection Regulation, that's the GDPR, and the Digital Services Act, or DSA.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=75)** Around the world, there are AI laws for governing the security of intellectual property, so it doesn't get unfairly sucked up into the training data of an AI; for watermarking items, so people know that it was created by an AI; and for harm reduction, especially in medical and military situations where people's lives are literally at stake.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=98)** These are changing on a constant basis, and it's hard to know exactly what's going to happen in 2025, 2026, and subsequent years, but it is continually evolving.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=111)** Next, there are the ethical issues; this is separate from legal.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=115)** It has to do with forms of fairness, so equity, equality, and need, which all refer to different ways of sharing things among people and making decisions.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=126)** There are forms of distributed justice, procedural justice, and interactional justice, all of which need to be respected to have the proper impact that you want.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=136)** And there are issues of authenticity. Who is speaking?
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=140)** How are you representing with other people?
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=144)** There are also social issues that are involved with AI and data science.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=148)** So for instance, it's important to engage with respect.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=152)** Be respectful of the people from whom you are gathering the data and to whom you are providing a service.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=157)** Remember, people don't like getting fooled or exploited or ignored.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=162)** And unpopular projects, while legal, can lead to protests and walkouts that become very costly, both in terms of lost time and lost energy for an organization.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=174)** Now, I gave you just a very quick overview of some of the potential issues.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/legal?u=76281980&t=179)** If you want more information on these, better examples, and how to work through them, another course here on LinkedIn Learning is AI Accountability Essential Training, which addresses these issues in greater depth and will help you keep your work on the right side of the law on ethical issues and social issues.

>

>

>

>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=170)** Now, I'm just going to mention for a moment that my academic training is in research psychology, and trying to understand what's going on in an artificial brain so you can explain it sounds an awful lot like the job we have trying to figure out what's going on in a biological brain.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=187)** The short version is it's really complicated.

>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=212)** And it hopes to unite the astounding success of algorithms like neural networks with the legal and commercial and social need for understanding.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=222)** Now, there are three main criteria for XAI algorithms.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=226)** The first one is transparency.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=228)** Essentially, this means that you can see the process happening, like going from a black box to a glass box.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=235)** So you can see how the model parameters are extracted from training data and how labels are generated for the testing data.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=243)** Next is interpretability.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=245)** This is the ability to identify the underlying basis for decision-making in a way that's understandable to humans.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=254)** It's also been described as the ability to determine cause and effect from machine learning models.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=261)** And then the third one is explainability, which refers to how the features in a model contribute to a decision, or more specifically what a given node in a neural network, the little circles here that are connected, what a given node in a neural network represents and how it contributes to the overall model, the output there at the bottom of this one.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=286)** Now, something that I really want to point out is this is aspirational.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=290)** Explainable AI is a goal.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=294)** It doesn't refer to any particular algorithm.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=297)** There's not like a deep learning neural network and then an explainable AI network.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=301)** It's a way of describing things.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=304)** And the trick here is that the goal of explanation can conflict with the goals of speed and accuracy.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=311)** So it's the attempt to strike the balance.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=315)** But there are several useful methods for approaching this.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=318)** One, for instance, is the use of algorithms that are more transparent.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=323)** So things like linear regression, that's usually easily interpretable, or a decision tree like we see here, or maybe collections of decision trees.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=333)** Those are things that people can understand better, and they are still often very effective.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=340)** There's also research in developing what you can call parallel algorithms in that you have one algorithm that is doing the main work, but next to it and at the same time you have another algorithm that is working to explain and follow the process what's going on in there.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=358)** Think of it as an explainable version of generative adversarial networks where you have two networks working simultaneously to produce a particular outcome.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=368)** And then there's also the general approach of what's called variable importance, and a lot of measures use that.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=375)** They may not be able to say specifically that this data point triggered this, but they say the model in general relies heavily on this data, less heavily on this data.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=386)** Which gets to the idea that there are existing solutions, but they are generally proprietary versions of XAI.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=394)** So for example, IBM has Watson OpenScale, and it can do a lot of this work.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=400)** It has visualization and a short, computer-generated text description of the variable importance in the model.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=407)** It also has something called contrastive explanation, and that is the minimum change required in the data to change the predicted outcome.
>
> **[6:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=417)** That's a nice way of thinking about it.
>
> **[6:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=419)** And not surprisingly, there are similar offerings from other cloud computing providers.
>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=423)** This is a very hot market.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=425)** A lot of people are working in it.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=427)** Then again, sometimes you just want to simplify things a little bit.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=430)** So for instance, there is an algorithm called COMPAS, C-O-M-P-A-S, and that's a proprietary black box algorithm that is sometimes used for recidivism or re-offending risk predictions in, for instance, deciding whether somebody gets to go on parole or not.
>
> **[7:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=449)** And the trick is you don't know what it's in there because it's a black box of some kind.
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=454)** Also because it's proprietary, the people who made it, they're not telling anybody what's in there.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=460)** And the problem is that there are identified, well-known biases and really some issues with the COMPAS program.
>
> **[7:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=470)** And so a collection of researchers from Berkeley, Harvard, and Duke developed something called CORELS, which is a portmanteau for certifiably optimal rules lists.
>
> **[8:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=481)** And what this is, is like a decision tree, but they were able to develop a very short list of nested if-then statements that happen to match the performance of two-year recidivism risk predictions from the proprietary black box compass algorithm.
>
> **[8:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=501)** And there's this only four lines.
>
> **[8:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=503)** It says first, if the offender's age is between 18 and 20 and they are male, then predict that they're going to re-offend.
>
> **[8:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=511)** Well, that's easy.
>
> **[8:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=512)** Else, if they are a little older, 21 to 23, and if they have two to three prior offenses, but sex is no longer in the equation, then predict yes, they will re-offend.
>
> **[8:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=525)** Or, a little simpler, if their number of prior offenses is greater than three, regardless of their age or sex, then predict yes.
>
> **[8:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=533)** And anybody who's not covered any of those, predict no.
>
> **[8:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=537)** Four lines, extremely effective and very, very interpretable.
>
> **[9:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=543)** Now the other problem is it's also not really satisfactory 'cause you think, "Man, there's a lot more things that should go into a sentencing thing."
>
> **[9:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=550)** But what this does is to highlight that if these four lines can match the predictive accuracy of some proprietary black box algorithm, then maybe we don't want to be using that particular algorithm.
>
> **[9:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=563)** Maybe we want to try something that does get a little more of the nuance to the things that are important in our lives.
>
> **[9:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=570)** Now, what all of this means, it's a long road.
>
> **[9:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=573)** We're not there yet, but the need for explainable AI is abundantly clear and there's some promising progress towards the goal.
>
> **[9:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/explainable-ai?u=76281980&t=581)** Hopefully in the not too distant future, we'll see some advances that unite the extraordinary abilities of AI algorithms with the transparency, interpretability, and explainability that all of us need.

#### Agency of algorithms and decision-makers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] When we think about artificial intelligence, and how it works, and how it might make decisions, and act on its own, we tend to think of things like this.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=9)** You've got the robot holding a computer right next to you, but the fact is, most of the time when we're dealing with artificial intelligence, it's something a lot closer to this.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=19)** Nevertheless, I want to suggest at least four ways that work in data science can contribute to the interplay of human and artificial intelligence of personal and machine agency.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=33)** The first is what I call simple recommendations, and then there's human-in-the-loop decision making, then human-accessible decisions, and then machine-centric processing and action.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=45)** And I want to talk a little more about each of these.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=48)** Let's start with recommendations.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=50)** This is where the algorithm processes your data and makes a recommendation or a suggestion to you, and you can either take it or leave it.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=58)** A few places where this approach shows up are things like, for instance, online shopping where you have a recommendation engine that says, "Based on your past purchase history, you might want to look at this."
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=68)** Or the same thing with online movies or music.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=71)** It looks at what you did, it looks at what you like, and it suggests other things, and you can decide whether you want to pick up on that or not.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=79)** Another one is an online newsfeed that says, "Based on what you've clicked in the past and the things that you've selected, you might like this."
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=86)** It's a little bit different because this time it's just a yes or no decision, but it's still up to you what you click on.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=91)** Another one is maps, where you enter your location and it suggests a route to you based on traffic, based on time, and you can follow it or you can do something else if you want.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=102)** But in all of these, data science is being used to take truly a huge amount of information about your own past behavior, about what other people have done under similar circumstances, and how that can be combined to give the most likely recommendations to you.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=118)** But the agency still rests in the human.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=121)** They get to decide what to do.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=124)** Next is human-in-the-loop decision making.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=128)** And this is where advanced algorithms can make and even implement their own decisions, as with self-driving cars, and I remember the first time my car turned a steering wheel on its own, but humans are usually at the ready to take over if needed.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=143)** Another example might be something as simple as spam filters.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=145)** You go in every now and then and you check up on how well it's performing.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=149)** So it can do it on its own, but you need to be there to take over just in case.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=156)** A third kind of decision-making in the interplay between the algorithm and the human is what I call human accessible decision making.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=165)** Many algorithmic decisions are made automatically and even implemented automatically, but they're designed such that humans can at least understand what happened in them, such as, for instance, with an online mortgage application, you put the information in and it can tell you immediately whether you're accepted or rejected.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=184)** But because of recent laws, such as the European Union's GDPR, that's the General Data Protection Regulation, the organizations who run these algorithms need to be able to interpret how it reached its decision, even if they're not usually involving humans in making the decisions, it still has to be open to humans.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=204)** And then finally, there's machine-centric, and this is when machines are talking to other machines.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=210)** And the best example of this is the Internet of Things, and that can include things like wearables.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=216)** My smartwatch talks to my phone, which talks to the internet, which talks to my car, and sharing and processing data at each point.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=224)** Also, smart homes, you can say hello to your smart speaker, which turns on the lights, adjusts the temperature, starts the coffee, plays the news, and so on.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=233)** And there's smart grids, which allows, for example, for two-way communication between maybe a power utility and the houses or businesses they serve.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=241)** It lets them have more efficient routing of power recovery from blackouts, integration with consumer-generated power and so on.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=248)** The important thing about this one is this last category, the machine-centric decisions or the Internet of Things is starting to constitute an enormous amount of the data that's available for data science work.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=259)** And that's to say nothing about the progress that's happened with generative AI, where it really is taking charge and acting like an agent.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=268)** So think about, for instance, AI-generated content, articles that you might read online or potentially reviews of items, they should be written by humans, but are sometimes by AI.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=279)** Images and posts on social media, or sometimes people use the AI to create the work that they submit to their supervisors, and then maybe there's even reviews submitted by the supervisors.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=291)** So the AI can be involved in both elements of it.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=294)** Now, if you want the worst-case scenario version, I'm a teacher, and in schools, you have the risk of teachers using generative AI to create their assignments, and then students using AI to write their papers, and then teachers using their AI to grade the papers, and then students using AI to argue about their grades, and then teachers using AI to defend their grades and so on and so forth.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=318)** Obviously that's taking the agency several steps too far in the wrong direction.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=323)** But the general idea there is that with generative AI, we actually do have AI that can exercise agency ideally under human control to reach the goals that were important to the humans who asked them for it.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=338)** But throughout all of this, one really important element is authenticity.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=344)** You still want to be yourself.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=345)** Now, remember the entrepreneurial rule of eliminate, then automate, then delegate.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=350)** The idea is make your life easy by eliminating everything that's not directly relevant to your goals.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=356)** Automate as much as you can of the things that you don't eliminate, and of the things that can't be eliminated or automated, delegate as much as you can.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=365)** And the rule there is if somebody else can do the job 80% as well as you would, delegate it.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=373)** And so that's something that is usually used to talk about working with humans who are helping you.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=380)** But the same thing can be true of using AI.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=384)** But it all does come at a cost.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=386)** When you hand off too much to another person or to an AI, you run the risk of losing your voice, your own humanity, and your authenticity.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=397)** Use the AI, use the algorithms to assist you in your work.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=401)** But above all, don't lose yourself in the process.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=405)** Remember, you are the one who has the voice, who has the perspective and has the potential to contribute something wonderful.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/agency-of-algorithms-and-decision-makers?u=76281980&t=412)** Use your tools to help you with that, but maintain your humanity throughout it.

### 5. 4. Sources of Data and Insights

#### Data preparation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] Anybody who's cooked knows how time-consuming food prep can be, and that doesn't say anything about actually going to the market, finding the ingredients, putting things together in bowls and sorting them, let alone cooking the food.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=14)** And it turns out there's a similar kind of thing that happens in data science, and that's the data preparation part.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=21)** The rule of thumb is that 80% of the time on any data science project is typically spent just getting the data ready.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=31)** So, data preparation, 80%, and everything else falls into about 20%.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=36)** And that can seem massively inefficient and you may wonder what is your motivation to go through something that's so time-consuming and really this drudgery?
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=46)** Well, if you want in one phrase, it's GIGO, that is garbage in, garbage out.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=53)** That's a truism from computer science.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=56)** The information you're going to get from your analysis is only as good as the information that you put into it.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=63)** And if you want to put in really starker terms, there's a wonderful phrase from Twitter that says, "Most people who think that they want machine learning or AI really just need linear regression on cleaned up data."
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=78)** Linear regression is a very basic, simple, and useful procedure, and it lets you know, just as a rule of thumb, if your data is properly prepared, then the analysis can be something that is quick and clean and easy, and easy to interpret.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=96)** Now, when it comes to data preparation in data science, one of the most common phrases you'll hear is "Tidy data," which seems a little bit silly, but the concept comes from data scientist Hadley Wickham, and it refers to a way of getting your data set up so it can be easily imported into a program and easily organized and manipulated.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=119)** And it revolves around some of these very basic principles.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=123)** Number one, each column in your file is equivalent to a variable, and each row in your file is the same thing as a case or observation.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=134)** Also, you should have one sheet per file.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=137)** If you have an Excel sheet, you can have lots of different sheets in it, but a CSV file has only one sheet.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=144)** And also that each file should have just one level of observation.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=149)** So, you might have a sheet on orders, another one on the SKUs, another one on individual clients, another one on companies, and so on and so forth.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=159)** If you do this, then it makes it very easy to import the data and to get the program up and running.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=167)** Now, this stuff may seem really obvious and you say, "Why do we even have to explain that?"
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=172)** It's because data in spreadsheets frequently is not tidy.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=177)** You have things like titles and you have images and figures and graphs, and you have merged cells and you have color to indicate some data value, or you have sub-tables within the sheet, or you have summary values, or you have comments and notes that may actually contain important data.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=196)** All of that can be useful if you're never going beyond that particular spreadsheet, but if you're trying to take it into another program, all of that gets in the way.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=205)** And then there are other problems that show up in any kind of data, things like, for instance, do you actually know what the variable and value labels are?
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=212)** Do you know what the name of this variable is?

>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=216)** Or what does a three on employment status mean?
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=220)** Do you have missing values where you should have data?
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=224)** Do you have misspelled text?
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=225)** If people are writing down the name of the town that they live in or the company they work for, they could write that really an infinite number of ways.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=233)** Or in a spreadsheet, it's not uncommon for numbers to accidentally be represented in the spreadsheet as text, and then you can't do numerical manipulations with it.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=243)** And then there's a question of what to do with outliers.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=245)** And then there's metadata, things like, where did the data come from?
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=249)** Who's the sample? How is it processed?
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=251)** All of this is information you need to have in order to have a clean dataset that you know the context and the circumstances around it that you can analyze it.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=262)** And that's to say nothing about trying to get data out of things like scanned PDFs or print tables or print graphs, all of which require either a lot of manual transcription, or a lot of very fancy coding.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=277)** I mean, even take something as simple as emojis, which are now a significant and meaningful piece of communication, especially in social media.
>

>

>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=293)** Here's a few of them.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=294)** And if you're going to be using this as information, you need to prepare your data to code all of these in one single way so that you can then look at these summaries altogether and try to get some meaning out of it.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=306)** I know it's a lot of work, but just like food prep is a necessary step to get something beautiful and delicious, data prep is a necessary, vital step to get something meaningful and actionable out of your data.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=321)** So, give it the time and the intention it deserves.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-preparation?u=76281980&t=323)** You'll be richly rewarded.

#### Labeling data for supervised learning
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Sometimes it's nice to know where you are and what's around you.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=5)** Now, I mentioned elsewhere that the supervised and unsupervised learning approaches differ primarily in terms of whether the data are labeled, and in a machine learning context, the labels are the outcomes that we care about, whether it's a category that you classify something into or a score of some kind that you're trying to predict.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=26)** But what those labels do, just like this sign, is they orient us in the data.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=32)** They give us a point of focus, like this art installation that's just a few blocks from my home in Salt Lake City.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=39)** But there's an art to labeling data just as an art to sorting your laundry so things don't come out with their colors all mixed up.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=47)** Except instead of sorting one basket of laundry, you might be trying to sort a hundred million cases, which is why you need to use something a little more robust than one item at a time.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=60)** Now, there are a few ways that you can get labels for your data.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=63)** The easiest is to use data that has labels already, like a financial dataset that already has all these successful loan payments already marked, or a biological dataset that has this species indicated already.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=76)** That's kind of like getting a special little gift because you can take it and you can get up and running on your project right away.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=84)** Or maybe you can do some kind of automatic labeling like the automated mail sorting here.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=89)** The trick is that A, somebody has to have already developed a method for creating those labels; and B, it's probably not going to be 100% accurate.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=99)** So you have to think about how much noise or how many misclassifications you can tolerate in your data.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=105)** And third, maybe you can turn to crowdsourcing, like a service like Mechanical Turk or CrowdFlower where you pay a lot of people a little bit of money to manually categorize data.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=117)** And I know that last one sounds really tedious and it's because it is tedious, but it's also the source of some of the best known datasets in the machine learning world.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=127)** For instance, ImageNet is a database that was created in 2007 for object recognition in digital images.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=136)** It consisted of people rating 3.2 million images just to say what was in the pictures.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=144)** There were over 20,000 different categories of things in those pictures and they were hand-labeled by nearly 50,000 people on Amazon's Mechanical Turk.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=156)** What this did is it gave the absolutely critical reference source to begin developing enormous models to deal with the complex issue of the categorization of images.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=171)** Now, another way to do this is CAPTCHAs or the completely automated public Turing test to tell computers and humans apart or the I'm a human thing.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=182)** So for instance, you are going to go log into something and it tells you, "Click all the images of shoes," and you got to click the two shoes and sometimes it'll ask you to do it again.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=193)** And what's going on here is that this kind of authentication task, which is again designed to tell whether you're a human, where they have people select the objects in the photos, they're not just finding out whether you're human, they're also gathering manual classification of photos for use in other tasks.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=214)** So it's kind of a to-for for them.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=216)** You're not aware that you're performing this crowdsourced work.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=219)** It's an example of what's called an embedded training task for embedded training data.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=224)** On the other hand, I will mention that there are some ethical issues when you have to keep doing it.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=229)** I've had to do caps three times in a row.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=232)** Or when it doesn't really seem necessary, it feels like somebody's kind of getting some free data from you.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=237)** But it is a way to get your categorized data.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=240)** Now, that's if you don't care who does the categorization.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=244)** On the other hand, there are times when the labels that you need to give are critical.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=249)** It can be a matter of life and death.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=251)** Like for instance, you need a cancer doctor, an oncologist to identify potential tumors in CT scans like we have here.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=259)** And in that case, because you need a very, very well-trained expert to rate the data and it can take a long time, and it's very important to be accurate, you can go through this by steps.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=272)** You can have a pre-training dataset that might have a very large number of cases that have each been reviewed only one time by one doctor.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=281)** So take it as the first estimate, understanding that any one doctor could make a mistake, could miss something or might be a difficult one.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=290)** Now the advantage of this is you can get a lot of data relatively quickly and then you can train your machine learning algorithm on this larger dataset with the weak labels, is the term.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=303)** Those are labels that are not 100% guaranteed to be accurate.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=306)** And then you can get another smaller dataset where, for example, maybe every case has been reviewed by multiple doctors where you can have very high confidence in the accuracy of the labels, and then that data set can be used to fine-tune the model that came from the first, maybe not 100% accurate dataset.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=327)** So again, you can go in successive approximation so you get something that you are very confident with.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=333)** Now all of this is separate from the other issue of bias in labeling.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=338)** This is a very big issue and it's much more than simply having noise in the data.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=342)** It's about missing the target entirely.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=345)** Now sometimes it's a technical issue, but sometimes it is something that has some very serious social repercussions.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=352)** And there are several things that can give rise to this.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=355)** One is, for instance, maybe you just have limited data that you're developing your algorithm with.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=362)** So the training data doesn't contain the full range of possibilities, like our semi-mythical black swan here.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=368)** Maybe you don't have the black swan in your training data 'cause black swans are very rare.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=372)** I don't think I've ever personally seen one.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=375)** But it's the sort of thing that could show up in the real data and your algorithm would miss it.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=381)** So you need to check whether certain groups or categories are underrepresented or even excluded from your data.
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=389)** You also have to watch out for the potential of confounded categories where even though this group always goes with this group, you need to be able to tell them apart separately.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=400)** And it functions differently inside the algorithm.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=404)** There's also the issue of limited or biased labels.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=408)** And this is where you have labels created by people with similar culture and expectations, so they may view things the same way.
>
> **[6:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=414)** Like for instance, the high-end glamping setup here with your very fancy canvas tent out in the woods.
>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=421)** Some people love this and they would be willing to pay a lot of money for it.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=425)** Others just want to run away screaming.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=427)** If you have a very similar group of people who are rating, for instance, accommodations and they all give this one a five 'cause it looks so wonderful, well, that's going to be biased 'cause not everybody wants it.
>
> **[7:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=438)** And sometimes these are just matters of individual differences, but they can also be cultural differences, either very small cultures or very large groups where you can't reliably expect these people to view things the same way as other people.
>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=455)** And when that happens, those labels that go into the machine learning algorithm, they can be biased in a way that makes the algorithm less useful for those other group of people or potentially outright misleading.
>
> **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=467)** And so the idea is that social judgements that happen at the labeling stage can get baked into the algorithms.
>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=474)** They become part of it.
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=475)** So you have a very significant responsibility to get accurate and sufficiently diverse data and labels for use in your algorithm.
>
> **[8:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=487)** The general rule that you've probably heard before is G-I-G-O, GIGO: garbage in, garbage out.
>
> **[8:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=493)** If you have bad data or restricted data or culturally-limited data going into your algorithm, you're not going to get very useful results.
>
> **[8:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=503)** You might be able to predict for that one group of people that you got the data from.
>
> **[8:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=506)** But generally when you send something out into the world, you're going to want it to be applicable to much more of the world, and so you need to have something that's much more diverse going in.
>
> **[8:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/labeling-data-for-supervised-learning?u=76281980&t=516)** And so the labels that you get become really a matter of both technical accuracy and of social justice, and it's something that fully deserves your attention.

#### In-house data

>

>

>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=126)** Dark data is data that does in fact exist, but isn't used to drive insights or make decisions or really isn't used at all.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=134)** And like the dark matter that is in the universe, and can't be seen directly, but it's hypothesized to constitute maybe 85% of the matter of the universe.
>

>

>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=164)** When asked about their data, they said approximately 12% of their data was actively used.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=172)** That is, they actually used it to get some insights and make some decisions.
