>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=187)** So it's data that exists, but it's kind of useless.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=191)** And the thing is, it costs money to have this out of date data around.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=196)** In fact, there's an estimate that the cost of this redundant, obsolete, and trivial data could be over $3 trillion.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=203)** And then finally, the largest chunk by far, 65% is dark data.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=208)** This is data that exist, but it's hidden within the networks or people or their own machines.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=214)** Now, other estimates put the percentage of dark data even higher from 90 to even 99% of all data.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=221)** And don't forget, this matters because A, it takes resources, and it takes money, and equipment to store the data, and there are also regulatory issues, and even some risks associated with having very large amounts of data around that you actually don't need for your current purposes.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=241)** Now, in terms of why people have this dark data, what the challenges are, one is an organization may not have the right tools to capture or to use the data.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=250)** 85% of people surveyed said that was one of the reasons that they couldn't work with it.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=256)** The flip side of that is, they've got too much data.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=259)** They are so overwhelmed with the big data fire hose coming in, and they don't have enough analytics or the ability to work with it.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=266)** 39% of people gave that as a reason.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=270)** Next is that they simply couldn't use the unstructured data.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=274)** Remember things like text, free text, the things that people put on social media or the reviews they put on an e-commerce site, that's valuable, but it is difficult to process, especially if you're working in Excel.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=289)** And then finally, there is the ever present risk that the data is dark because it's missing or incomplete, and simply not in a standard to be usable.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=298)** And 66%, two thirds of the people gave that as a reason for their dark data.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=303)** Now that said, there is some value in simply turning around, look around, see what's at your organization.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=312)** Chances are there is at least some data, possibly a very large amount that is already very well suited to the projects you have in mind to bring value to your organization.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/in-house-data?u=76281980&t=323)** And maybe with some extra work, with some extra exploration, you can find ways to bring in all of that extra data that currently is not being used, that's dark data to bring more insights, and more value to your data science work.

#### APIs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] When you draw a picture or write a letter, chances are that you can draw well with one of your hands, your dominant hand, and not so much with the other.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=9)** I recently heard someone describe this as having a well-developed API for your dominant hand, but only a clunky one for the non-dominant hand.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=18)** An API, or Application Programming Interface, isn't a source of data, but rather, it's a way of sharing data.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=25)** It can take data from one application to another or from a server to your computer.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=29)** It's the thing that routes the data, translates it, and gets it ready for use.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=34)** I want to show you a simple example of how this works.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=38)** And so I've gone to this website that has what's called the JSONPlaceholder.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=43)** JSON stands for JavaScript Object Notation.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=46)** It's a data format.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=48)** And if we scroll down here, you'll see this little tiny piece of code, and what it says is go to this web address and get the data there and then show it, include it, and you can just click on this to see what it does.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=62)** There's the data in JSON format.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=64)** If you want to go to just this web address directly, you can, and there's the same data.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=71)** You can include this information in a Python script or an R script or some other web application that you're developing.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=78)** It brings it in and it allows you to get up and running very, very quickly.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=83)** Now, APIs can be used for a million different things.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=87)** Three very common categories include social APIs that allow you to access data from Facebook or a Bluesky or other sources, as well as use them as logins for your own sites; utilities, things like Dropbox and Google Maps, so you can include that information in your own apps; or commerce: Stripe for processing payments or MailChimp for email marketing or things like Slack or a million other applications.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=110)** The data can be open, which means all you need is the address to get it, or it may be proprietary.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=115)** Maybe you have to have a subscription or you purchase it and then you'll need to log in, but the general process is the same.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=121)** You include this bit of code and it brings the data in and gets you up and running.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=126)** You can then use that data in data analysis, so it becomes one step of a data science project, or maybe you're creating an app.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=136)** You can make a commercial application that relies on data that it pulls from any of several different APIs like Weather and Directions.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=145)** Really, the idea here is that APIs are teamwork.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/apis?u=76281980&t=148)** APIs facilitate the process of bringing things together and then adding value to your analysis into the data science-based services that you offer.

>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/scraping-data?u=76281980&t=34)** It's when you take the data that's around you, tables on pages and graphs in newspapers, and integrate that information into your data science work.

>

> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/scraping-data?u=76281980&t=219)** It's not an enormous amount of work, and it is a way of recovering the data that was used to create a map like this.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/scraping-data?u=76281980&t=227)** If you want to see a really good example of data scraping after a manner where you're taking image data and getting something useful out of it, here at [publicintelligence.net](https://publicintelligence.net), they're reporting on a project that's done with Syrian refugee camps, and what they're using here are satellite photos, and then they're using a machine learning algorithm to count the number of tents within the camp, and that's important because, you see, as it goes over time, it gets much, much larger, and this can be used to assess the severity of a humanitarian crisis and really, how well you can respond to it, but it's the same basic principles of data scraping, taking something that was created in one way, just an image, but then using the computer algorithms
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/scraping-data?u=76281980&t=276)** to extract some information out of it and give you the ability to get some extra insight and figure out what your next steps in your project need to be.

>

>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=207)** These are for things that are very intricate, interdependent, and difficult to predict.
>

>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=224)** And so the ability to synthesize data that matches your needs for data science and AI in medicine is a great opportunity.
>

>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=255)** It's how they get AIs to do video games, is by having them run through this simulated environment.
>

>

>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=282)** Now, you may be familiar with the approach that's generally used called GANs, which is for generative adversarial network.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=292)** And the idea here is you get at least two AIs, one of which generates content, so it makes a new image.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=299)** So here, you can see on the right, we have these images that start with very low resolution, get higher resolution, to something that is eventually identifiable as an image.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=311)** That AI is the generator, it is creating new content.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=314)** It could be images, it could be text, it could be audio or video, it could be whatever.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=319)** It could even be a data set that matches the dimensions of your medical dataset.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=326)** And then a second AI, another one, which is called the discriminator, tries to discriminate generated from real content.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=333)** So one AI is throwing out a bunch of fake data along with real data, and when it gets to the point that the discriminator is no longer able to tell the difference, well, then you have managed to create some very convincing content.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=349)** It's how movies like the, quote-unquote, "live-action" "Lion King" were made by using the generative adversarial networks to get digital images that looked and moved like real animals.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=362)** And it's really effective in a lot of ways of getting past some of the obvious limitations of AI.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=368)** Now, there is one risk in all of this with the GANs, but even with the simulated and the synthesized data, and it goes way back to the ouroboros, and that is the ancient symbol of a snake or dragon eating its own tail.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=386)** The problem is that ais can start to consume their own content that was generated by AI, in order to model humans.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=394)** This is especially true when AI generates content that is then posted on the web, then they gets sucked up by other AIs to be used for training data.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=402)** And it runs the risk of AI feedback loops where it starts to really, instead of modeling what humans do, it says, what would an AI that is designed for a human do?
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=411)** And that's just a different problem entirely.
>
> **[6:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/synthetic-data-and-simulation-environments?u=76281980&t=414)** But the point here is that if used responsibly and thoughtfully, both synthesis and simulation of data can help create the kinds and the quantities of datasets that are needed for high-level data science and AI work.

#### Passive collection of training data

> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] Some things you can learn faster than others, and food is a good example.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=5)** A lot of people love eating mussels, but some people eat them, get sick, and then never want to touch them again, and I'm one of those people.

> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=67)** One is, for instance, photo classification.

>

>

>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=119)** All this information is gathered without you doing any extra work.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=123)** And if you're the provider of an app that measures health, you can get that information directly without having to do anything else for it.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=133)** Now, there's several benefits to this kind of passive collection of training data.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=138)** Number one is that you can get a lot of it.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=140)** You can get enormous amounts of data very quickly, simply by setting up the procedure and then letting it roll either automatically or outsourcing it to the people who use it.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=151)** The data that you gather can be either very general, or it can be very, very specific general, like categorizing photos as cats or dogs, or specific like knowing what a particular person's heart rate is at a particular time of day.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=165)** There are, on the other hand, some challenges associated with this passive data collection.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=170)** One, and this is actually a huge issue, is that you need to ensure that you have adequate representation, things like categorizing photos.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=179)** You need to make sure you have lots of different kinds of photos of lots of different kinds of things and different kinds of people, and so you can get all of those categorized and that your algorithm understands the diversity of the information it will be encountering.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=193)** You also need to check for shared meaning.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=196)** What that is, for instance, is that something being happy or beautiful?
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=201)** You need to make sure that people interpret that in the same way.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=205)** You also need to check for limit cases.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=207)** Think about, for instance, heart rate.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=209)** Some people are going to have just higher heart rate than others, and you don't want to have an algorithm that always says anything above this level is a heart problem, anything below is fine, because that is going to vary from one person to another and one situation to another.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/passive-collection-of-training-data?u=76281980&t=225)** But what all of this does together is it helps you assemble the massive amounts of data that you need to get critical work done in data science.

#### Data vendors

>

>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-vendors?u=76281980&t=289)** So, there are some significant advantages, but there are some significant costs, both literal financial costs, as well as things potentially like social goodwill.
>

>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-vendors?u=76281980&t=323)** But it also allows you to get up and running with your clientele as fast, and hopefully, as effectively as possible.

>

>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=175)** They're giving you something of value.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=177)** So make sure that you are respectful of that.

>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=183)** Just what is necessary to help you get the insights you need in your own particular project.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=189)** Now, another method of gathering data that can be extremely useful is an experiment.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=193)** Now I come from a background in experimental social psychology.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=197)** The experiments are very time-consuming, very labor intensive, but that's not what we're talking about in the e-commerce and the tech world.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=206)** Usually here we're referring to what's called A/B testing, where for instance, you prepare two different versions of a website or a landing page, and you see how many people click on things as a result of those two different versions.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=218)** That's extremely quick and easy and effective, and people should constantly be doing that to try to refine and focus on the most effective elements of their website.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=229)** But through all of this, there is one overarching principle.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=233)** When you're gathering data, when you're engaging with people, seriously, keep it simple.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=239)** Simpler is better, more focused is more efficient, and you're going to be better able to interpret it to get useful next steps out of the whole thing.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=251)** I also want to mention two elements of research ethics that are especially important when you are gathering new data.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=258)** Number one is informed consent.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=261)** When you're gathering data from people, they need to know what you want from them, and they also need to know what you're going to do with it so they can make an informed decision about whether they want to participate.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=273)** Remember, they're giving you something of value.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=275)** This is something that needs to be their own free choice.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=278)** The second one, which can be expressed in many different ways is privacy.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=283)** Also, sometimes confidentiality or anonymity.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=286)** You need to keep identifiers to a minimum.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=288)** Don't gather information that you don't need and keep the data confidential and protected.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=295)** Now that's actually a challenge because when you get this kind of data, it's often a value and it's easy for it to get compromised.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=302)** So maintaining privacy really is incumbent upon the researcher and it's part of building the trust and the good faith for people to work with you and others again in the future.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=315)** And then finally, to repeat something I said before, people are sharing something of value with you.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=321)** So it's always a good idea to show gratitude and response, both by saying thank you in the short term, and also by providing them with better, more useful services.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/new-data-from-surveys-and-experiments?u=76281980&t=333)** They're going to make things better in their own lives.

>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=50)** People must know what they're getting into so they can make a free voluntary choice to participate.
>

>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=74)** You try to make it so that people feel that their data is going to be private.
>

>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=87)** Confidentiality means, regardless of whether the data is anonymous or not, that people know you're not going to freely share the data that is going to stay in certain tight bounds.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=96)** I have a locked filing cabinet in my locked office, which is where I'm required to keep the data that I've gathered.

>

>

>

### 6. 5. Tools and Techniques for Data Science

#### Applications for data analysis

#### Languages for data science

>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/languages-for-data-science?u=76281980&t=46)** The reason this question comes up is because programming languages give you immense control over your work in data science.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/languages-for-data-science?u=76281980&t=54)** You may find that your questions go beyond the capabilities of data analysis applications.

> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/languages-for-data-science?u=76281980&t=71)** But let me say something more about Python and R.

> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/languages-for-data-science?u=76281980&t=109)** And R is still very popular with scientists and with researchers.

> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/languages-for-data-science?u=76281980&t=126)** And Python manages in-memory and large datasets better in its default setup.

> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/languages-for-data-science?u=76281980&t=198)** And often, it's the packages that are more influential than the actual language.

> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/languages-for-data-science?u=76281980&t=214)** But no matter what language you use and what packages you use, it is true that the programming languages that are used in data science are going to give you this really fine-level control over your analysis and let you tailor it to the data and to the question that you have.

#### MLOps
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A lot of data work is done on an ad hoc basis, one project at a time by one person at a time.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=7)** That's certainly the case in my background, which is academic research.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=10)** In that respect, it's a little like making fabric or rugs on a hand loom one line of thread at a time.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=18)** It can be beautiful, but it's also really time consuming.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=23)** And if you're trying to produce thousands of rugs or thousands of yards of fabric, you need to scale up to a well developed and largely automated approach, so you can both meet the demand and ensure quality at that level of output.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=39)** In the software world, the corresponding approach that aims to get products out at scale and with constant improvement is known as DevOps, which is a combination of development or the programming part and operations, which is the information technology part that supports the lifecycle of the product.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=60)** A similar approach is used for products like social media sites and consumer data products.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=65)** Think, for instance, of fitness trackers or online chatbots that are data products, meaning they rely on incoming data in order to produce the outcome.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=75)** In this case, the approach is referred to as MLOps or machine learning operations.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=83)** Now, the beautiful thing about this approach is that it really facilitates integration.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=88)** There's a lot of different people who need to be collaborating on your machine learning project, data scientists, machine learning engineers, software developers, IT operations, and MLOps is a way of getting all of their different roles organized, their roles identified, and gets things coordinated.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=106)** In addition, there's the technical aspect.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=108)** Maybe you have onsite computing and maybe you have cloud computing systems, and maybe you even have edge or on-device machine learning on a person's phone or in sensors, and you need to integrate both the programming for these and the data coming in from each of them to make your algorithms work well.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=127)** MLOps makes this much easier.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=132)** MLOps also addresses quality by looking at continuous integration, or CI, and continuous deployment, or CD.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=141)** Not surprisingly, if you're familiar with Agile methodologies, that's a big part of what goes into both DevOps and into MLOps.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=151)** It makes it easier, for instance, to be responsive to changes in the regulations and consumer preferences and behaviors, the competitive environment, staffing, any number of issues.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=161)** And so the agile and continuous approach to quality and MLOps makes for a better product.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=170)** Now, in the course of this, you'll come across a couple of things very, very often.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=174)** One is the concept of containers, and this is an environment, Docker is the most common, that makes your code portable across different machines, even different operating systems.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=186)** Makes it scalable, that you can ramp it up very quickly, and it's very speedy to troubleshoot, to implement it, to work with it.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=196)** And so a containerized development system like Docker is very common in MLOps.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=203)** And in addition to that, if you have a lot of different containers, then you're going to need a method of orchestrating or organizing the work between the containers.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=211)** And a very common one is Kubernetes, which coordinates the deployment, the scaling, and the management of container-based systems in MLOps, the same way it does in DevOps.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=223)** The beautiful thing about all of this is it makes it so that your systems, instead of being 1% at a time, can be automated as much as possible.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=233)** So you can have systems that tell you when you need to update your algorithms, when you need to check the output.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=241)** It also makes it scalable, so you can take it from just a small number of users or organizations to a very large number very, very quickly and easily.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=251)** And it makes it easier to develop systems that are explainable because you have this consistency and you understand better what's going into things, and the collaboration between the different people is much clearer and more organized.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/mlops?u=76281980&t=265)** It gives you a better high-quality product, and so when you're working in a large scale development environment with a lot of people, and you're working with many different systems, these very basic concepts from DevOps as adapted to data science and machine learning operations can be a wonderful way to organize your projects and get things running up quickly at scale and at high quality.

### 7. 6. Math Foundations for Data Science

#### Sampling and probability
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] AI and data science are amazing, but things don't always go as expected.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=7)** Algorithms crash or can produce severely biased results that lead to very embarrassing PR problems for your organization.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=15)** And part of this problem, especially with bias, can be traced to sampling issues.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=22)** Now, if you want to have a valid and generalizable result, one of the things that you need to have, not the only one, but one of them is a representative sample.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=34)** And the easiest way, at least in theory, to get a representative sample is through simple random sampling, which is not to be confused with haphazard or convenience sampling.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=46)** A simple random sample draws from a large population, say for example, a complete list of customer transactions or social media followers, and draws from that list with an equal probability for every case.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=60)** This is done repeatedly until you have the number of observations that you need for whatever procedure.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=67)** If you do this, there is a high probability, it's not 100%, but often close enough, that whatever you find in your sample data will generalize well to the population or the entire group that you drew your data from.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=84)** A related procedure is used whenever people do cross validation or hold out validation, where sampling is used to separate the data into, for example, training and testing sets for analysis and verification.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=99)** The idea there is that the model developed with one randomly selected dataset will also function well with the others, and so sampling is important for that.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=112)** On the other hand, something you need to know about sampling and probability, you know that if you flip a coin 10 times and you do that repeatedly and count the number of heads you get each time, you won't always get the same number.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=127)** Even though the coin is the same, it hasn't changed, and also the probability of getting heads hasn't changed, it's going to be 50% each time.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=137)** Anyone who has done cross validation or holdout validation knows that you never get exactly the same model and the same accuracy from one random subset to another, but hopefully they're close.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=151)** And if you use enough data, if you have a large-enough sample to begin with, they should be very close.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=160)** But this only applies if you have the variability in your data to begin with.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=167)** Which brings up another important point about new populations.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=171)** The other thing about variability is stuff that never got captured and put into your dataset in the first place.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=179)** When a company branches out to a new product line or to a new market, they're going to a kind of terror incognito.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=186)** You can't automatically assume that the models you've made for one will transfer to the other.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=193)** You may have a great understanding of how a social influence works on, maybe Twitter, but that may not transfer well to TikTok.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=201)** Or you may have an excellent model for predicting the purchasing behavior of 30-somethings on the Pacific Coast, but it may not work well with teens in the Gulf Coast, let alone with grandparents in Central America.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=214)** The best algorithm in the world can't predict something that just doesn't appear in your data, which means it is incumbent on you to spread your net wider to make sure you actually have data that captures the variability that you care about.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=232)** Also, you need to qualify your results.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=235)** All of this should serve as a reminder that the results of any analysis or model are inherently probabilistic.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=244)** They are not guaranteed to be exactly the same, except maybe in the case of a highly controlled simulation, but that's not what you were looking for in the first place.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=254)** Every model is an approximation of reality.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=259)** It's a model that responds to that, but there's always some slippage.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=265)** So there's a discrepancy, and this slippage is something important to keep in mind as we talk about some of the mathematics behind data science.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=273)** So we don't automatically fall into the idea that if there's an equation and we're obviously dealing with something absolute and indisputable, keep in mind that you need to still be circumspect, you need to be humble.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=288)** And sampling, which introduces the limits to our analyses just as through the process of partitioning datasets, but it lets us know that everything has some built-in inescapable variability that we're going to have to acknowledge and include in our conclusions so that we can have a sample model that generalizes and is more applicable to a larger population without getting us in trouble by going too far.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/sampling-and-probability?u=76281980&t=319)** Keep your limits in mind and you will be able to deliver better value in your data science.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** case. (1), this, (1), from. (1), function (1), let (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1), make (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (2)
> **Prerequisites:** you need to have (1)

#### Algebra
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Lecturer] When you're working with data, it's not too hard to come up with a solution when you have only one problem at a time and it's basically stationary, but it's a whole different story when you have thousands of problems impeding at you at the same time.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=16)** In that case, you need a more flexible approach, and this is where algebra can come to the rescue.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=24)** There are two reasons that it's important to understand algebra and data science.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=27)** Number one is it allows you to scale up.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=30)** The solution you create to a problem should deal efficiently with many instances at once.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=36)** Basically, create it once, run it many times.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=40)** And the other one closely related to that is the ability to generalize.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=44)** Your solution should not apply to just a few specific cases with what's called magic numbers, but to cases that vary in a wide range of arbitrary ways.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=54)** So you want to prepare for as many contingencies as possible.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=59)** And so we'll start with the basic building blocks of data science, which is elementary algebra.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=66)** An algebraic equation looks like this.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=69)** This in fact is a linear regression equation, but what we're doing is we're using letters to stand in for numbers.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=75)** That actually is an interesting thing because a lot of people think that people who work in mathematics and work in data work with numbers all the time.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=82)** We actually work with variables, and so this is the association between variables.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=88)** So let's start right here.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=89)** Here on the far left, y is the outcome and the subscript i means is for the case i person or observation i, that could be one or two or a thousand or a million.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=99)** Right next to that is a Greek letter that's a lowercase beta, like a B, and it has a zero because it is the y intercept.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=108)** It's sort of the starting value before we add anything else.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=112)** Next to that is another beta, but this time with a sub 1, this is a regression coefficient and it's the slope that we use for this variable X1.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=122)** So that's going to be our first predictor variable, and we're going to multiply it for the value of case i.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=129)** Then we do similar things with the second regression coefficient and the second predictor variable.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=135)** Then the third regression coefficient and the third predictor variable.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=138)** And here at the end, we have an epsilon, and this stands for error, and it means how far off is our prediction from the actual values for each person one at a time.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=150)** And as wonderful as a regression equation like that is, the power of computing comes in when we go past a single dimension to the rows and columns of a matrix.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=159)** That's how your computer likes to see math and how it processes it.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=164)** This is what's known as linear algebra.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=167)** It works with matrices and vectors.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=171)** Over here on the far left is a vector that has all of the outcome scores for each case.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=176)** In this situation, there's only two.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=178)** There's y1 for the first person and y2 for the second.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=182)** If there were a hundred people, we would have a hundred numbers all arranged vertically.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=187)** If there were a million, we'd have a million numbers arranged all vertically.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=190)** Those are the outcomes and they're in a vector.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=193)** Right next to this is all the scores.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=195)** This is a matrix because it has both rows and columns and it contains the data for each individual person.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=203)** Next to that is another vector, which has the regression coefficients written again with the beta, and we have the intercept at the top and then each of the three slopes.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=214)** And then we finish with a vector of error terms for each individual person.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=220)** There's only two in this case, but there could be a thousand or a million.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=224)** Let me fill this in with some numbers so you can see how it works in practice.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=228)** If we're going to estimate the salary for a person working in data science, and this is actually based loosely on real data from a few years ago, let's say we have two people, and the first one has a known salary of 137,000, the other one has a salary of 80,000.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=244)** And what we're going to do is see how we can run that through this matrix equation, this linear algebra, to estimate those numbers.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=253)** The first thing here is we have their data in the matrix on the left and the regression coefficients on the right.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=259)** This one is the intercept.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=261)** Everybody gets a one because everybody's number is multiplied times 50,000.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=265)** That's the starting value that everybody gets.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=268)** Next to that is a number that indicates years of experience.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=271)** So this person has nine years, and for each year we estimate an additional $2,000 in salary.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=279)** Next to that is a number that indicates negotiating ability on a one to five skill, where one is very low, very poor negotiator, five is a very strong negotiator.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=288)** And for each step up, we predict a $5,000 increase in annual salary.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=294)** This person has a three as their middling, so we would add $15,000 onto their expected salary.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=302)** This last one is an indicator variable.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=303)** It says 0, 1.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=305)** 0 is no, 1 is yes to say whether this person is a founder or an owner of the company.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=310)** If they are, then we would expect them to make about $30,000 more per year.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=314)** That's reasonable.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=315)** And when you put all of these things together, we predict 113,000 for this first person.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=321)** Now they actually had 137,000, so we're off by a little bit.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=325)** And that's the error equation.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=327)** And that doesn't necessarily mean we messed up.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=328)** We only have three variables in the equation.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=331)** We didn't put down where they live, we didn't put down what sector they're working in, and we didn't put down what kind of client projects they have, although those things would influence it as well.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=341)** But this shows you how you can use these vectors and matrices to do the algebra for an important question like estimating salary.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=352)** Now, one of the neat things about matrix notation is that it's like packing a suitcase.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=357)** You can go from lots of different individual things into a much more compact and maneuverable and agile package.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=366)** This is matrix notation, and what it is here is this is the same information that I showed you just a moment ago, except now you see how it's in bold.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=375)** Each of these symbols now stands for the entire vector or matrix.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=379)** This Y on the side stands for every single outcome score for every person in our data.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=386)** This X is the matrix of all of the predictor data, and this bolded beta is the vector of all of the regression coefficients.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=395)** And this over here, the epsilon, is the vector of all error terms.
>
> **[6:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=399)** So it's very compact, and this is the way the computers like to deal with the information and it makes it much easier for them to manipulate it and get the things that you're looking for.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=409)** Now, even though you're going to be using the computer to do this, there's a couple of reasons you want to be aware of how the algebra functions.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=416)** Number one is it allows you to choose procedures well.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=420)** You can note which algorithms will work best with the data that you currently have to answer the questions that motivated your project in the first place.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=430)** And the second one is it can help you resolve problems.
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/algebra?u=76281980&t=432)** Things don't always go as planned, and you know what to do when things don't go as expected so you can respond thoughtfully and get the insight and the actionable steps you need out of your data.

#### Calculus
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/calculus?u=76281980)

> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/calculus?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] You may have the best product or service in the world, but if you want to get paid, you've got to make the sale and you've got to do it in a way that's profitable for you.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/calculus?u=76281980&t=10)** Surprisingly, calculus may be one of the things to help you do just that.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/calculus?u=76281980&t=16)** The idea here is that calculus is involved any time you're trying to do a maximization and minimization when you're trying to find the balance between these disparate demands.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/calculus?u=76281980&t=26)** Let me give you an example of how this might work.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/calculus?u=76281980&t=28)** Let's say that you sell a corporate coaching package online, and that you currently sell it for $500, and that you have 300 sales per week.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/calculus?u=76281980&t=38)** That's $150,000 revenue per week.

>

>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/calculus?u=76281980&t=58)** So the idea here is you can change the sales by adjusting the price, but where are you going to have the maximum revenue?

#### Bayes' theorem
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] Performing is hard.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=3)** You put in a lifetime of training, weeks of preparation for an event, and even when you've done the very best, you can never be completely certain that everything is going to work out exactly the way you wanted it to.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=14)** There's a certain element of luck or probability associated with it.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=18)** It's the same thing in data science.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=21)** No matter how big your dataset, no matter how sophisticated your analysis and the resources available to your organization, there's still an inescapable element of probability.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=31)** It's just how it works.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=32)** And one of the best things you can do is to explicitly incorporate that uncertainty into your data science work to give you more meaningful and more reliable insights.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=45)** This is Bayes' theorem, and this is one of the keys to incorporating that uncertainty.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=50)** What Bayes' theorem does is it gives you the posterior, or after the data, probability of a hypothesis as a function of the likelihood of the data given the hypothesis, the prior probability of the hypothesis, and the probability of getting the data you found.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=67)** Now, you can also write it as a formula like this, but it's going to be a lot easier if I give you an example and do the work graphically.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=75)** So let's take a look at medical diagnosis as a way of applying Bayesian analysis or Bayes' theorem to interpreting the results of a study.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=87)** There are three things you need to know.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=88)** First off, you need to know the base rate of a disease.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=91)** How common is it overall?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=93)** Let's assume that we have a disease that affects 5% of the people who were tested.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=98)** Then we need to know the true positive rate.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=101)** So 90% of the people with the disease will test positive.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=105)** It means that 10% won't.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=106)** Those will be false negatives.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=109)** There's also a false positive rate.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=112)** That's 10% of the people who do not have the disease will also test positive.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=117)** It depends on how the test is set up.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=120)** But that's not completely unlikely.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=123)** And so we have the base rate, the true positive rate, and the false positive rate.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=127)** We can use those to answer this question.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=130)** If a person tests positive, and the test is advertised as 90% accurate, then what is the probability that they actually have the disease?
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=140)** Well, I'm going to give you a hint.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=141)** The answer is not 90%.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=144)** And it has to do because of the way we incorporate the base rates and the false positives.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=150)** So let's go and look at this.

>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=185)** Now, next to that, we add the people without the disease who test positive.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=190)** That's 10% of the 95%.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=193)** So that's 9.5% of the total.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=195)** And those are the false positives.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=198)** And so you can see, everybody in blue got a positive result, but what's the probability that you actually have the disease?
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=204)** Well, to do that, we're going to calculate the posterior probability of the disease.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=209)** We take the true positives and divide it by all of the positives, the true and the false positives.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=215)** Now, in this particular case, that's 4.5% divided by the sum of 4.5 and 9.5%, which is 14%.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=223)** 4.5 divided by 14% is 32.1%.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=227)** And what this means is that, even though the test is advertised as 90% accurate, depending on the base rate of the disease and the false positive, a positive test result may still mean that you have less than a 1/3 chance of having the disease.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=243)** That's a huge difference between what people expect the result to be and how it's actually going to play out in practice.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/bayes-theorem?u=76281980&t=250)** And that's one of the most important things you can do for accurately interpreting and putting into action the results of your data science analyses to get meaningful and accurate insight.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (3), function (1), this, (1), this. (1), case, (1)
> **Versions:** 4.5 (4), 9.5 (2), 32.1 (1)
> **Definitions:** means that (1)
> **Prerequisites:** set up (1)
