### 8. 7. Learning Paradigms

#### Supervised, unsupervised, and reinforcement learning
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] People can be very enthusiastic about data science, and sometimes a little too enthusiastic, and they sometimes seems to confuse data science with magic.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=11)** But as it turns out, data science doesn't involve genies in a bottle, it doesn't involve fairy dust.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=17)** Instead, it involves algorithms and machine learning.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=23)** Algorithms are just a series of decisions, not too different from this flowchart.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=28)** In fact, it's those algorithms that make machine learning possible, or rather, machine learning is the ability of a computer program to find patterns in a data all on its own without you specifically saying, "If it's red, do this, if it's green, do this."
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=46)** But it sees what the pattern is without being explicitly programmed, And this is where some of the most important developments in data science have happened.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=57)** And for our purposes, I want to briefly introduce three versions of machine learning, three general categories.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=66)** The first is supervised learning.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=68)** This is where you are classifying new cases into pre-existing labeled categories.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=74)** It's like taking a piece of mail and deciding which mailbox it goes into, and this is one of the biggest and most productive areas of data science.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=85)** It's simply the prediction of defined outcomes as well, like if you know a person's blood test score, you might try to get other variables about their behavior that can predict their score on this outcome.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=98)** So you have a defined category or outcome.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=102)** And supervised learning can involve methods as simple as regression, logistic regression, or linear regression, or something as complicated as a deep learning neural network.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=114)** Next is unsupervised learning.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=117)** And this is where you will simply find similarities without established labels or scores.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=123)** So you don't have a single outcome.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=125)** You're just putting like with like, like sorting fruit, put the same kind with each other.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=131)** So good examples of this are the various forms of clustering methods or dimensionality reduction, where you have a lot of different variables in your dataset and you're trying to reduce it to a smaller composite amount to make your life a little easier.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=145)** Also, anomaly detection.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=147)** Finding things that stick out from the pattern, possibly as something going wrong with the system or maybe as an indicator of a niche, where there might be more value that you haven't explored.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=159)** And then the third general category is reinforcement learning.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=164)** And these are algorithms that help computers to play Go.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=168)** They help robots run parkour and cars that drive themselves.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=172)** Now, understand it's not that we have this one set outcome, not putting things into a category, but simply the algorithm gets rewarded, it gets points, it gets scored for a better performance than it did before, and it learns how to do more and more of what it is it's getting rewarded for.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/supervised-unsupervised-and-reinforcement-learning?u=76281980&t=192)** And so between those three general categories, between supervised learning, where you're classifying into known categories or scores, unsupervised learning, where you're simply trying to find the similarities within the data and reinforcement learning, where you're giving it rewards for performing a given task better, that summarizes some of the most important and magic-like elements of data science.

>

>

>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=239)** We have six scores down here.
>

>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=242)** So the median is 3.5.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=245)** That splits the dataset into two equal halves.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=248)** Or you have the mean.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=249)** This one actually has a formula, which means the sum of X divided by N.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=254)** It also has a geometric expression.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=257)** The mean is actually the balance point.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=258)** If you put these as actual boxes on a seesaw, the mean is where it's going to balance, and in this case, it's exactly at 4.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=266)** It's going to rest flat at that point.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=269)** And so these are very common procedures.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=272)** I imagine you know them already, but think of them as a good place to start when you're looking at your data.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=279)** And if you can choose a second number to describe your data, you should consider a measure of variability, which tells you how different the scores are from each other.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=288)** So that can include things like the range, which is simply the distance between the highest and lowest score, the quartiles or IQR, which split the data up into 25% groups, the variance and the standard deviation, two very closely related measures that are used in a lot of statistics.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=305)** And you will also want to look at associations.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=309)** And so for instance, this is a scatterplot that shows the association between the psychological characteristic of openness and agreeableness at a state-by-state level.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=319)** You can look at some measures that give you a numerical description of association, like the correlation coefficient, regression analysis, like I just barely showed you, or depending on your data, maybe an odds ratio or a risk ratio.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=333)** But remember, there's a few things.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=334)** The data that you're analyzing must be representative of the larger group you're trying to understand.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=340)** And things like the level of measurement, is it nominal, ordinal, interval, or ratio, is going to have an impact on what measures you use and the kinds of inferences you can make.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=350)** You always need to be attentive to the effect of outliers.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=353)** You have one score that's very different from all the others 'cause that's going to throw off a of these measures.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=358)** Also, open-ended scores where you have like 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 plus or undefined scores where somebody started something but didn't finish can also have a dramatic effect on the data.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=369)** So you want to screen your data for these things.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=372)** Now, I can't go into the detail of all of these things right here, but we do have other courses here that can do that for you, such as Data Fluency: Exploring and Describing Data.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/descriptive-analytics?u=76281980&t=382)** Go there, go to the other courses available that give you an introduction to these basic concepts of understanding what's going on in your data and describing the patterns that you can find, so you can get started on the further exploration of your data science analysis.

#### Clustering techniques
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/clustering-techniques?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/clustering-techniques?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Everybody in a crowd is their own person.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/clustering-techniques?u=76281980&t=4)** Each person is a unique individual, and perhaps in an ideal world, your organization would acknowledge that and interact with each person in a tailored and unique way.

>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/clustering-techniques?u=76281980&t=98)** If you have 500, then you have 500 dimensions.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/clustering-techniques?u=76281980&t=102)** What you need to do then is you need to find a way to measure the distance between each point, and you're going to do one point every other point and you're looking for clumps and gaps.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/clustering-techniques?u=76281980&t=112)** You can measure distance in a lot of ways.

> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/clustering-techniques?u=76281980&t=113)** You can use Euclidean distance.

#### Dimensionality reduction
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Back in 1957, in his legendary song, "Rock and Roll Music," Chuck Berry sang a lament about musicians who make things too complicated, and to quote, "Change the beauty of the melody until it sounds just like a symphony."
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=15)** And that's why he loves rock and roll music.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=19)** The same idea explains why most bands have four people, like The Beatles right here, or possibly three or five.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=26)** That's enough people, enough instruments to fill all the sonic regions without overwhelming with too much information and resulting in cacophony.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=38)** Jumping way ahead in time, there's a similar problem in data science.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=43)** We think of data coming down in a matrix-like stream here as really cool, but it's hard to get meaning out of it, and it's hard to know what to do as a result of it.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=54)** We need a way to get through the confusion and the haze and pull things into focus.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=60)** Fortunately, there's a way to do that in data science.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=64)** The idea of dimension reduction is to actually reduce the number of variables and the amount of data that you're dealing with.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=71)** So, instead of dealing with dozens or hundreds, or maybe even thousands of variables, you're dealing with a single score, like how likely a person is to behave in a particular way.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=83)** It sounds counterintuitive, but there are actually some very good reasons for doing this.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=88)** First off, each variable, each factor or feature, has error associated with it.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=94)** It doesn't measure exactly what you want.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=96)** It brings in some other stuff, but when you have many variables or features together that you combine, the errors tend to cancel out.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=104)** So, if they're all pointing in slightly different directions, you end up centered on what it is you want.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=109)** Also, by going from many individual measurements to a single conglomerate measurement, that reduces the effect of something called colinearity, which is the association, the overlap between predictor variables in the model, which creates some significant problems.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=124)** And so, if you have fewer variables, there's less problems for colinearity.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=129)** Also, not surprisingly, when you have a few features you're dealing with instead of hundreds, you are able to do things faster.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=137)** Your computer's able to process the information with greater speed.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=141)** And another really nice consequence of this is it improves generalizability.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=146)** Again, because you're getting rid of or averaging out the idiosyncratic variation with each observation with each variable, and you're going to get something much more stable that you're able to apply to new situations better.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=162)** Now, there are two general ways to do this.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=165)** There are a lot more options, but the two most common are these.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=168)** Number one is called principal component analysis, often just called principal components, or PCA.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=174)** And the idea here is that you take your multiple correlated variables and you combine them into a single component score.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=182)** So, let's say you give a personality questionnaire and it's got 50 questions on it, but you have 10 questions for each element of personality.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=191)** Then, you can combine those into 10 components, if the analysis supports that combination, and then you only have five things to deal with as opposed to 50.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=201)** That's much easier to deal with.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=203)** Another very common approach is factor analysis.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=207)** And functionally, it works exactly the same way.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=211)** People use it for the same thing.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=212)** Although the philosophy behind factor analysis is very different.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=216)** Here, your goal is to find the underlying common factor that gives rise to multiple indicators.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=222)** So, in principle component analysis, the variables come first and the component results from it.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=228)** In factor analysis, this hidden factor comes first and it gives rise to the individual variables.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=234)** That said, even though they're conceptually very different that way, people tend to use the two often interchangeably.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=241)** What they let you do is group variables in ways that make sense.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=247)** And not surprisingly, AI approaches work into dimension reduction as well.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=252)** Generative AI approaches, as I've mentioned elsewhere, thrive on high-dimensional data.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=258)** Again, not just dozens or hundreds, but potentially billions of variables or fields.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=265)** Some of the most important approaches that are based on AI really transformer methods are variational autoencoders, or VAEs, as a common approach for working this kind of high-dimensional data.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=279)** Like I've mentioned elsewhere, they are context aware.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=282)** What that means is they're able to look at a huge amount of data around it and they're able to come up with simulated understanding and meaning.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=290)** And once you build these models, one really peculiar benefit of them is you can have what's called zero-shot learning that, especially a foundational model, which is built about all sorts of information.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=305)** Think of the general GPT model.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=307)** Basically, you know, is about everything that's been fed into it.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=311)** It can cluster immediately based on the data that it already has.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=318)** A couple of other perks of using these gen AI or transformer-based models is that they are built for non-linear data, and so they're able to model non-linear associations and use those when they're clustering.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=333)** They can also work at much higher dimensions.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=336)** and that allows them to, again, use things like multimedia images, audio, video, and not just text or numbers.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=345)** Also, they're able to do probabilistic models where they're able to transform each of the individual data points from just a point in a multidimensional space to a probability distribution, which allows it to do an entirely different way of measuring the overlap between points and finding the common dimensions between them.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=366)** But remember a few things.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=370)** AI methods are generally more computationally intensive.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=374)** There's a lot of power, there's a lot of computing that goes into them.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=378)** Also, you may lose interpretability, again, 'cause it's very much a black box method, in order to gain the power and the flexibility.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=387)** On the other hand, one of the huge benefits is that these generative AI methods, you know, you can talk to them.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=394)** You can say in words, you can speak out loud to tell it what you want, and it can talk back to you and tell you what's going on.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=401)** It can give you narrative explanations of the dimensional models that it's building.
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=406)** So, these are some enormous strengths in terms of creating and interpreting and even applying the results of a dimensional analysis.
>
> **[6:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=415)** Right now, I want you to know that there's the possibility of doing this and that it's worth looking into.
>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=422)** I mean, think about it.
>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=423)** Dimension reduction in data is like learning to read a language.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=428)** At first, you just see random shapes.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=430)** Then, you see individual characters.
>
> **[7:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=433)** Then words, then sentences.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=435)** And then finally, ideas.
>
> **[7:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/dimensionality-reduction?u=76281980&t=436)** You can go from a hundred pieces of information, a line here, a circle there, to just a handful, and that's what you need to get meaning out of your data and to do something useful with it.

#### Trend analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you go out hiking in a new place, it's nice to have a path to help you get where you're going.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=6)** It's also nice to know if that path is actually going to take you someplace you want to be.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=11)** As a data scientist, your job is to figure out what path the data is on so you can inform decisions about whether to stay on the current path or whether changes need to be made.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=23)** The most basic way to do this is to take your data over time and make a line chart.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=28)** It's a graph of those changes over time.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=31)** Connect the points to make a clear line, and maybe you've got something that's interpretable right off the bat.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=37)** On the other hand, when you're looking at changes over time, one of the things you need to be aware of is autocorrelation, and this is a situation where every value at each point in time is influenced by the previous values, more or less.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=52)** So it doesn't suddenly go from being 80 degrees one day to 20 degrees the next day to 112 the next day.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=59)** The temperature of each day is associated with what the temperature was the day before, and in most time-series, what you're looking for is consistency of changes, say, for instance, on a percentage basis.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=74)** You may also be trying to figure out what the actual function is, the shape of the curve.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=80)** So you're trying to get a mathematical formula of function that describes the curve.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=86)** It might be cyclical, something that has a seasonal variation.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=91)** It might actually be several functions at once that you have to combine, but I can show you some of the simplest ones.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=97)** The easiest one by far is linear growth.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=100)** It's the same quantity is added every time period, like dollars per hour or per year for an employee.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=107)** Or you can have exponential growth, where instead of going up the same number of units at each step, it goes up the same percentage at each step.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=116)** If you're dealing with, say for instance, the number of followers on social media, at a certain point it's going to look like this.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=123)** If you're looking at the growth of a stock, it usually looks something like this.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=127)** You can also have logarithmic trends that rise rapidly at first, but slow down as it approaches a ceiling, like operating at 100% capacity if the initial growth is fast.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=139)** Or a sigmoid, a logistic function, which is used in a number of algorithms.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=144)** It starts slowly, it accelerates, then tapers off as limits are reached, such as markets reaching saturation for a new product.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=152)** And then there's sinusoidal or a cyclic sign wave.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=157)** This is something that goes up and down over time, again, like a seasonal variation, the temperature or the bending on trips to go to the beach over time.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=169)** And there's several options for looking at these variations in trend analysis.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=174)** They include methods like time-series decomposition, ARIMA models, and even neural networks, where you're looking for substantial and perhaps even qualitative changes over time, like a flock of birds that's moving, perching, moving, and so on.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=190)** But let's look at this first method where we're going to try to break things down into their elements to see what's happening with your data over time.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=198)** The first method is decomposition.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=201)** This is where you take a trend over time and you try to break it down into its several constituent elements.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=208)** Now that includes both an overall trend, say for instance, think of a stock price.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=212)** Is it generally uphill?
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=214)** But it can also include a seasonal or cyclical trend as well as some random noise.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=221)** And so here's a graph that's looking at a stock market index over time from 1990 up to about 2017, and you can see it's generally uphill and it's got a lot of bounciness going on in there.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=235)** Well, we can do a decomposition, and what that does is it takes our dataset.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=241)** This is a compressed version of what we saw just a second ago, and it smooths it out to give us an overall trend.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=248)** You can see it's basically going from the bottom left to the top right.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=251)** It's uphill, it's got a few bumps in there, but it's generally uphill.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=256)** This third section is the seasonal trend that's looking at every year.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=260)** It seems to go up a certain amount, back down, then up and then back down.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=265)** Now, there are certain situations where this is going to make a lot of sense.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=268)** It depends on what this stock market is actually looking at and how things react in terms of how interpretable this method is, but you can see that there is a seasonal element to it.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=279)** And then the last one is the random element that says once you take this smooth general trend and you take the seasonal trend, this is what's left over, and you can see it goes from about 1,000 points down to about 1,000 points up.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=294)** And so it's something that matters, but if you go back to the top one, you can see that those are just the little squiggly variations on it.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=302)** This is a way of decomposing a trend, breaking it down into constituent elements to see the overall trend, the cyclical part, as well as the noise that's left over.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=314)** A more advanced approach is something called ARIMA.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=317)** That stands for autoregressive integrated moving average model.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=323)** It's a mouthful, which is why people normally just say ARIMA.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=327)** The auto regressive or AR part means that later values in the time-series are predicted by earlier or lagged values.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=336)** The I in ARIMA is the absolute values are replaced by difference values.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=342)** This is the integrated part, and then the MA at the end, that means that regression errors are linear combinations of current and previous values.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=352)** That's the moving average part.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=355)** Now, there are a lot of variations on this.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=357)** There's ARMA, which doesn't have the I for the different scene.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=360)** There's SARIMA and CERAMAX which includes seasonal components, among others, but ARIMA is a great way of breaking down the data by looking back, looking over your shoulder to see what happened in the past to try to predict what's going to happen in the near future.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=376)** So here's a data set, pretty well known.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=379)** It's about monthly international air passengers going from about 1948 up to about 1963 or 4, and so this is when air travel was still pretty uncommon, but you can see that it's generally uphill, and it also looked like there's a big seasonal trend.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=397)** Now, the thing that you don't want to do is just stick a regression line through the data 'cause you're missing a huge amount.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=402)** You can get the overall trend, you know, if you have to describe it in three words, but you're missing a lot.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=409)** And so what I did instead is separated the last three years from the earlier years, used an ARIMA model, actually a sub-ARIMA because it had the seasonal component, to estimate the trend over time and then project it into the future.
>
> **[7:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=426)** And you have both the confidence intervals there in the gray as well as a very close match between the predicted values and the observed values, which are shown in the red and the blue, and so it's able to capture both the general upward trend as well as the seasonal variation.
>
> **[7:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=444)** So when you're working with time-series data, some variation of an ARIMA model is almost guaranteed to be part of your approach to understanding what's happening with that data.
>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=455)** On the other hand, you can also use neural networks, a tool of choice in the data science world these days.
>
> **[7:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=461)** When you're working with time-series data, two of the most common choices are a recurrent neural network, an RNN, or a multi-layer perceptron, an MLP, and because they're neural networks, you have an input layer, you have one or more hidden layers, and you have an output layer that says what the predicted values are going to be for that point in time.
>
> **[8:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=484)** Now, the thing about this is neural networks use non-linear activation functions, which again makes it possible for them to capture some unusual variation and trends in the data.
>
> **[8:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=495)** So here's the same dataset with airline passengers over time and in the black up to about 1960, that's the training data, and then I actually tried three different versions of neural networks.
>
> **[8:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=508)** This one is in fact an MLP, a multi-layer perceptron, but you can see the very close alignment of the predicted and the observed values in the red and the blue where a neural network is able to get something that's even more precise than what we had with the ARIMA model, and again, because you so often want to have multiple perspectives, it would be worth your while to do a decomposition and an ARIMA model and maybe some kind of neural network to try to get multiple views on the same phenomenon to give you more confidence in what you're doing.
>
> **[9:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=542)** Then again, data science is a very fast moving field, and especially when you're trying to do something like predict stock market prices, a lot of people are going to spend a lot of effort trying to get just a little bit more advantage.
>
> **[9:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=553)** And so even though neural networks have been developed over the last few years, and they're enormously powerful, there are also generative AI methods that have been enormously influential in the last couple of years.
>
> **[9:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=564)** Specifically, these are transformer-based models and they can build on the foundational models.
>
> **[9:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=571)** Think again, massive general purpose models like GPT-4 or Llama or Claude that are good with multiple timelines and can learn from just a few examples of the current data, because they already have this huge data store.
>
> **[9:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=586)** Now, the two methods that are most common for doing time-series analysis with transformers are not, surprisingly, one called the time-series transformer, or a TST model, and another one is a temporal fusion transformer, or TFT.
>
> **[10:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=603)** These are models that have been specifically trained for time-series data, but they're also good at dealing with temporal dependencies and dealing with missing data and some of the other things that can complicate a regular analysis.
>
> **[10:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=616)** Also, because generative AI and transforming methods are so good at having these really long contexts, really big windows that they're focusing on, these methods, the TSTs and the TFTs, are also good for really long sequences.
>
> **[10:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=631)** So if you have a list that's miles long, it's going to be able to help you sort through that, even if it's piling all over the floor.
>
> **[10:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=641)** In addition, even though causality is philosophically not possible with 100% certainty, there are attempts to use transformer-based models for causal inference, often by specifically incorporating counterfactual reasoning in the models.
>
> **[10:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=656)** That is, if we were to change this thing over here just a little bit, would we get the same outcome, or if we change this thing here, trying a little bit of if/then modifications to see how it affects the model.
>
> **[11:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=669)** Again, it's still not 100%, but because of the extraordinarily broad range that it's working with, it's a very, very good estimate.
>
> **[11:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=678)** Now that said, let's remember a simple principle that you've heard in economics in a lot of other places, is KISS, keep it simple, or really keep it simple, stupid.
>
> **[11:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=688)** That's the second S.
>
> **[11:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=690)** Simpler models like ARIMA and RNNs still matter.
>
> **[11:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=694)** They're very good when there is clear seasonality.
>
> **[11:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=698)** If things go up and down at the same time each year, they're very helpful.
>
> **[11:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=703)** Also, they involve a lot less computation than a generative transformer-based model does.
>
> **[11:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=709)** And especially with ARIMA models, well, they're relatively easy to understand.
>
> **[11:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=715)** I mean, you can graph it out, you can decompose it, you can see what's going on, and that's a huge advantage when trying to make sense and interpret what's going on in trends so you can do something useful with it.
>
> **[12:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/trend-analysis?u=76281980&t=728)** But what all of these approaches have in common is that they allow you to take what's behind you and they give you confidence to move forward.

#### Aggregating models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There's a saying that life imitates art.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=3)** Well, for some time, as I've been preparing for this presentation, I planned to talk about how people estimate the amount of money in a jar of coins.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=12)** And then it turned out that our family inherited a giant jar of coins from my mother-in-law, and we've asked everybody in our extended family for their guesses as to how much money is in the jar.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=25)** I guessed $165, but my wife, who was much more optimistic, guessed $642.50.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=32)** Well, we eventually took the jar to the money counter, and it turned out that it had $476 in it, which, as it happens, is almost exactly halfway between the guesses that I made and my wife made.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=47)** Any one guess, it turns out, is like an arrow shot at a target.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=52)** It may be high or low, it may be more or less accurate, but as it happens, if you take many guesses and average them like averaging the guesses that my wife and I each made, the errors tend to cancel out, and you end up with a composite estimate that's generally closer to the true value than anyone guesses.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=70)** And that was true in our situation.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=74)** This is sometimes called the wisdom of the crowds, although in statistics, it's a function of something called the central limit theorem and the way sampling distributions behave.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=84)** But the idea is that combining information is nearly always better than individual bits and pieces of information.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=93)** In data science, the wisdom of the crowd comes in the form of combining the results of several different models of the same dataset trying to predict the same outcome, like maybe a linear regression and a lasso regression and a decision tree and a neural network, again, all using the same data and all predicting the same outcome.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=113)** Now, one reason why it can be helpful to combine estimates is because just as some people will underestimate the number of coins in a jar like I did, and some people overestimate like my wife did, machine learning algorithms can also miss the mark in a few ways.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=128)** There can be underfitting or bias, and that's where your model is too simple and it loses key detail in the data.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=136)** It doesn't work to simply say, "Well, if you have this many years of education, your expected income is this level."
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=143)** That's just a straight ahead regression line, and it turns out that it misses a huge amount of the variation in the data, and it doesn't generalize well.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=151)** You may develop the model on your training data, but it just kind of falls flat when you try to apply it to a larger dataset.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=159)** The flip side of underfitting or bias in your machine learning algorithms is overfitting, where you have the problem of variance, and this is where your model is too complicated, and it follows every twist and turn in the data and matches it too closely, and it turns out that that also doesn't generalize well.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=181)** You get a model that has a thousand different parameters when you're trying to predict something that only should involve four things.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=189)** It doesn't serve the purpose of simplifying, which is the general goal of analysis.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=194)** And again, it doesn't go well, it doesn't translate well from your training data where you built a model to the real world.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=201)** You can think of it this way.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=203)** Here we have a little scatterplot that has a predictor variable across the bottom and output variable on the side, and you can see that it's generally uphill, but it curves down at the end on the right side.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=214)** Well, the underfit, the bad model here that is underfit and has too much bias, it just draws a straight line.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=222)** And yeah, there is a straight trend in there somewhere, but it's missing the curve in there.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=228)** On the far right, we have an model that has been overfit.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=233)** It's got too much variance where we got a line that's looping through every single point.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=238)** And so yeah, the line connects all the points, but there's no way it's going to work that exact same way with more data from the same general population.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=247)** Instead, you want the one that's in the middle.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=249)** That is an optimal level that balances between the bias of the underfit model and the variance of the overfit model.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=258)** That generally comes through the middle and it shows you that there's that dissenting curve at the end.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=263)** Another way to look at it is this model, which shows the model complexity across the bottom.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=267)** Think of just a straight linear regression on the left and some super complicated neural network on the right, and then the prediction error is going up the side where more error is bad.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=279)** And what you have is the very, very simple model which has high bias.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=284)** It's got a lot of error at the bottom because it's too simple, but it drops down, that's the blue line dropping down.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=291)** On the other hand, a model with high variance, it gets more and more error as the model gets more complex.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=299)** And so ideally, what you're going to do is you're going to take a function of those two curves, the blue descending one for bias, the red ascending one for variance, combine them and you get this kind of smooth U-shaped curve in yellow, and you want to pick the part where that is the lowest.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=320)** So you're going to pick a level of model complexity that gives you the lowest possible value for prediction error based on the methods that you applied to your data.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=333)** Now, this trade-off between bias and variance gives rise to the saying, "No free lunch," which in the machine learning world means that no one algorithm is always going to be the best.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=348)** No one choice will always minimize the error and give you the best solution to the bias variance trade-off.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=357)** On the other hand, there are several things that you can do to try to get a good solution to this by combining the estimates.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=364)** So for instance, bagging or bootstrap aggregating where you pull multiple random samples from your training dataset, you build several iterations of the same algorithm like a decision tree to predict the outcome on different samples from the training data.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=379)** Then combine the estimates, perhaps by using the most common outcome category, or averaging estimates where it's like voting.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=386)** You can also have boosting.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=388)** This is a sequential process where in the first step, a model is built, for example, to classify cases.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=394)** In the second step, a model is built for the cases that the first step got wrong, the misclassifications, and it goes at each step building new models for the cases that the previous model missed.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=408)** And then finally, they're stacking.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=410)** This is a way of using several different algorithms like logistical regression and k-nearest neighbors, a decision tree, and a neural network to classify cases.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=418)** Then using a higher level algorithm to build a weighted model that uses the predictions of those first level models to come up with an overall prediction.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=428)** All three of these approaches, bagging, boosting, and stacking, have proven to be very effective under different circumstances for combining different predictions to get the wisdom of the crowd in data science.
>
> **[7:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=444)** And when it comes to integrating generative AI models, well, you've got some choices there too.
>
> **[7:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=450)** One interesting one is contextual merging.
>
> **[7:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=453)** This is where the different models that are going to be aggregated can be chosen and weighted by context.
>
> **[7:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=462)** That is, it can understand what it is you're trying to accomplish and the situation that you're working in, and it can actually choose more of this model, less of this one, depending on what you're doing.
>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=474)** It includes something called prompt-based model selection, where it actually looks at the text that you enter in as a prompt in terms of what it is you're trying to do.
>
> **[8:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=485)** Also, you can have dynamic weighting where models can actively get more or less weight based on their history of performance in other situations.
>
> **[8:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=496)** And so the first one is based on what you're telling it right now.
>
> **[8:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=499)** The second one is based on the AI's evaluation of previous performance on its own.
>
> **[8:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=505)** And then there's always the perk of foundational models.
>
> **[8:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=508)** These models, again, like GPT-4, have broad contextual awareness.
>
> **[8:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=513)** I mean, again, they basically know everything that's available on the internet for rare cases for small groups, and they're able to explain their choices.
>
> **[8:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=523)** And so there's a lot that can be done with AI models when it comes to aggregating the different models you have in your analysis.
>
> **[8:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=533)** Now there's some tremendous benefits to combining models in this way.
>
> **[8:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=537)** The first is that you get multiple perspectives, several different ways of looking at the data.
>
> **[9:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=541)** Again, no one's system is going to be ideal for everything, and so it behooves you to try several different models to see how well they work with a particular task at hand.
>
> **[9:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=554)** And by doing so, they allow you to find the signal amid the noise.
>
> **[9:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=559)** And the reason it does that is because generally, each system will bring its own noise, but it will cancel out with the next one.
>
> **[9:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=567)** And what you are left with is something that is usually more stable and more generalizable.
>
> **[9:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=574)** The idea is that by combining the estimates you get from each of these models, you are able to find an optimal solution through the bias-variance trade-off.
>
> **[9:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=586)** And so you can think of it as a kind of cooperation between the models.
>
> **[9:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/aggregating-models?u=76281980&t=591)** The idea that many eyes on the same problem can lead to the best possible solution, which is why you got involved in data science in the first place.

>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=151)** And so, even though you feel like your logic might be airtight, you got to check it out with a lot of different variations.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=161)** Now, one very common way to do this is a validation process where you take a data set, a large data set, and you split it up, and you have training data, which is where you build the model.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=174)** That's where you try to understand the relationship between the factors in your data to allow you, for instance, to predict a score or classify a case at the end.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=186)** You can also have what's called cross-validation, where you take your dataset that you're training with, and then you split that up into five or six different parts.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=195)** And then you take four of the parts to model what might be happening with the fifth one, and then you rotate through.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=203)** And so every section of the training data has a chance to be the outcome that the rest of them are trying to predict.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=210)** This is considered a very good strategy.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=212)** It allows you to deal with some of the variation that's inherent in your data.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=216)** And then you can have holdout validation, when you first split your data into training data, which maybe you then split into cross-validation data, you also created another partition of your data that never got used in the developing of the models.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=232)** That's called the holdout.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=234)** That's the testing data.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=236)** And the idea is you develop your model with the training data.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=240)** Hopefully you went through cross-validation as well.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=242)** And when you think you've gotten something that hits the mark, you test it on the holdout data, and that gives you the best impression of how well your model will generalize to new situations.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=254)** You can also think of in the bag and out of the bag data, which is something that applies in certain algorithms.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=261)** So, in the bag data is the data that is used to build a model.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=265)** It's what you are working with at this exact moment.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=268)** And in bagging algorithms, or bootstrapped aggregates, these are randomly selected cases from your overall data that you used to build the model.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=276)** And it's often used with random forest models or collections of decision trees.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=282)** So, in the bag data is the data that is used to build the current model.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=289)** And separate from that, you have out of bag data that you can use to validate.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=293)** These are data points that were not randomly selected when getting the data to build the model.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=300)** And then what you can do is that for each data point that was out of the bag that wasn't included when you built that particular decision tree, you can check the predictions of all of the trees that don't use that particular point.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=314)** And then from those various predictions, you can see how well they match up with the true state, the actual classification of the point, and you can calculate the out of bag error, the OOB error.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=326)** And this is a great way of validating some approaches, again, particularly with random forest, a very, very common approach within data science.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-science-foundations-fundamentals-24591071/validating-models?u=76281980&t=336)** And once you do that, you can be certain that your work does what you think it should do, and you can be confident that it's ready to go out there into the real world.
